      <p>
       <b>
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         português do Brasil
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Portuguese" title="Help:IPA/Portuguese">
         [poʁtuˈɡez du bɾaˈziw]
        </a>
       </span>
       or
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         português brasileiro
        </span>
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Portuguese" title="Help:IPA/Portuguese">
         [poʁtuˈɡez bɾaziˈlejɾu]
        </a>
       </span>
       ) is a set of
       <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">
        dialects
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese language
       </a>
       used mostly in
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       . It is spoken by all of the 200 million inhabitants of Brazil
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and spoken widely across the
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_diaspora" title="Brazilian diaspora">
        Brazilian diaspora
       </a>
       , today consisting of about two million Brazilians who have emigrated to other countries.
      </p>
      <p>
       This variety of the Portuguese language differs, particularly in phonology and prosody, from the dialects spoken in
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese-speaking_African_countries" title="Portuguese-speaking African countries">
        Portuguese-speaking African countries
       </a>
       . In these latter countries, the language tends to have a closer connection to contemporary European Portuguese, partly because Portuguese colonial rule ended much more recently in them than in Brazil. Despite this difference between the spoken varieties, Brazilian and European Portuguese differ little in formal writing
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1990, the
       <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Portuguese_Language_Countries" title="Community of Portuguese Language Countries">
        Community of Portuguese Language Countries
       </a>
       (CPLP), which included representatives from all countries with Portuguese as the official language, reached an agreement on the reform of the Portuguese orthography to unify the two standards then in use by Brazil on one side and the remaining
       <a href="/wiki/Lusophone" title="Lusophone">
        Lusophone
       </a>
       countries on the other. This spelling reform went into effect in Brazil on 1 January 2009. In Portugal, the reform was signed into law by the President on 21 July 2008 allowing for a 6-year adaptation period, during which both orthographies co-existed. All of the CPLP countries have signed the reform. In Brazil, this reform will be in force as of January 2016. Portugal and other Portuguese-speaking countries have since begun using the new orthography.
      </p>
      <p>
       Regional varieties of Brazilian Portuguese, while remaining
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">
        mutually intelligible
       </a>
       , may diverge from each other in matters such as vowel pronunciation and speech intonation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_language_in_Brazil">
        Portuguese language in Brazil
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Portuguese language in Brazil">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The existence of Portuguese in Brazil is a legacy of the
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Portuguese colonization of the Americas">
        Portuguese colonization of the Americas
       </a>
       . The first wave of Portuguese-speaking immigrants settled in Brazil in the 16th century, but the language was not widely used then. For a time Portuguese coexisted with
       <a href="/wiki/L%C3%ADngua_Geral" title="Língua Geral">
        Língua Geral
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       —a
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       based on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerindian_languages" title="Amerindian languages">
        Amerindian languages
       </a>
       that was used by the
       <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">
        Jesuit
       </a>
       missionaries—as well as with various
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Africa" title="Languages of Africa">
        African languages
       </a>
       spoken by the millions of
       <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_Brazil" title="Slavery in Brazil">
        slaves brought into the country
       </a>
       between the 16th and 19th centuries. By the end of the 18th century, Portuguese had affirmed itself as the national language. Some of the main contributions to that swift change were the expansion of
       <a href="/wiki/Colonization" title="Colonization">
        colonization
       </a>
       to the Brazilian interior, and the growing numbers of Portuguese settlers, who brought their language and became the most important ethnic group in
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Beginning in the early 18th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">
        Portugal
       </a>
       's government made efforts to expand the use of Portuguese throughout the colony, particularly because its consolidation in Brazil would help guarantee to Portugal the lands in dispute with
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       (according to various treaties signed in the 18th century, those lands would be ceded to the people who effectively occupied them). Under the administration of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sebasti%C3%A3o_Jos%C3%A9_de_Carvalho_e_Melo,_Marquis_of_Pombal" title="Sebastião José de Carvalho e Melo, Marquis of Pombal">
        Marquis of Pombal
       </a>
       (1750–1777), Brazilians started to favour the use of Portuguese, as the Marquis expelled the Jesuit missionares (who had taught Língua y the
       <a href="/wiki/France_Antarctique" title="France Antarctique">
        French
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       during the 16th century and the
       <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Brazil" title="Dutch Brazil">
        Dutch
       </a>
       in the Northeast during the 17th century had negligible effects on Portuguese. The substantial waves of non-Portuguese-speaking immigrants in the late 19th and early 20th centuries (mostly from
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italy
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">
        Spain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">
        Germany
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
        Poland
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">
        Lebanon
       </a>
       ) were linguistically integrated into the Portuguese-speaking majority within few generations, except for some areas of the three southernmost states (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parana_(state)" title="Parana (state)">
        Paraná
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">
        Santa Catarina
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
        Rio Grande do Sul
       </a>
       )—in the case of Germans, Italians and Slavs—and in rural areas of the state of
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       (Italians and Japanese).
      </p>
      <p>
       Nowadays the overwhelming majority of Brazilians speak Portuguese as their mother tongue, with the exception of small, insular communities of descendants of European (German, Polish, Ukrainian, and Italian) and Japanese immigrants – mostly in the
       <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_Brazil" title="Regions of Brazil">
        South and Southeast
       </a>
       – as well as villages and reservations inhabited by
       <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">
        Amerindians
       </a>
       . And even these populations make use of Portuguese to communicate with outsiders and to understand television and radio broadcasts, for example. However, there is a community of
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Sign_Language" title="Brazilian Sign Language">
        Brazilian Sign Language
       </a>
       users whose number is estimated by
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">
         Ethnologue
        </a>
       </i>
       to be as high as 3 million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Loanwords">
        Loanwords
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Loanwords">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The development of Portuguese in Brazil (and consequently in the rest of the areas where Portuguese is spoken) has been influenced by other languages with which it has come into contact, mainly in the lexicon: first the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerindian_languages" title="Amerindian languages">
        Amerindian languages
       </a>
       of the original inhabitants, then the various
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_languages" title="African languages">
        African languages
       </a>
       spoken by the slaves, and finally those of later European and Asian immigrants. Although the vocabulary is still predominantly Portuguese, the influence of other languages is evident in the Brazilian lexicon, which today includes, for example, hundreds of words of
       <a href="/wiki/Tupi%E2%80%93Guarani_languages" title="Tupi–Guarani languages">
        Tupi–Guarani
       </a>
       origin referring to local flora and fauna; numerous
       <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">
        West African
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_language" title="Yoruba language">
        Yoruba
       </a>
       words related to foods, religious concepts, and musical expressions; and English terms from the fields of modern technology and commerce. Although some of these words are more predominant in Brazil, they are also used in Portugal and other countries where Portuguese is spoken.
      </p>
      <p>
       Words derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Tupi_language" title="Tupi language">
        Tupi language
       </a>
       are particularly prevalent in place names (
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Itaquaquecetuba" title="Itaquaquecetuba">
         Itaquaquecetuba
        </a>
        ,
       </i>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pindamonhangaba" title="Pindamonhangaba">
         Pindamonhangaba
        </a>
        ,
       </i>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Caruaru" title="Caruaru">
         Caruaru
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Ipanema" title="Ipanema">
         Ipanema
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Para%C3%ADba" title="Paraíba">
         Paraíba
        </a>
       </i>
       ). The native languages also contributed the names of most of the plants and animals found in Brazil (and most of these are the official names of the animals in other Portuguese-speaking countries as well), including
       <i>
        arara
       </i>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Macaw" title="Macaw">
        macaw
       </a>
       "),
       <i>
        jacaré
       </i>
       ("South American
       <a href="/wiki/Caiman" title="Caiman">
        caiman
       </a>
       "),
       <i>
        tucano
       </i>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Toucan" title="Toucan">
        toucan
       </a>
       "),
       <i>
        mandioca
       </i>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">
        cassava
       </a>
       "),
       <i>
        abacaxi
       </i>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Pineapple" title="Pineapple">
        pineapple
       </a>
       "), and many more. However, many Tupi–Guarani
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toponyms" title="Toponyms">
        toponyms
       </a>
       did not derive directly from Amerindian expressions, but were in fact coined by European settlers and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">
        Jesuit missionaries
       </a>
       , who used the
       <i>
        Língua Geral
       </i>
       extensively in the first centuries of colonization. Many of the Amerindian words entered the Portuguese lexicon as early as in the 16th century, and some of them were eventually borrowed into other European languages.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_languages" title="African languages">
        African languages
       </a>
       provided hundreds of words as well, especially in certain semantic domains, as in the following examples, which are also present in Portuguese:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Food:
        <i>
         quitute
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Quindim" title="Quindim">
          quindim
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Acaraj%C3%A9" title="Acarajé">
          acarajé
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Moqueca" title="Moqueca">
          moqueca
         </a>
        </i>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        Religious concepts:
        <i>

        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Macumba" title="Macumba">
          macumba
         </a>
        </i>

        <i>

        </i>
        ("
        <a href="/wiki/Orisha" title="Orisha">

        </a>

        </i>

        <i>

        </i>
        ("caress on the head"),
        <i>

        </i>
        ("
        <a href="/wiki/Joker_(playing_card)" title="Joker (playing card)">

        </i>
        ("youngest child", also
        <i>

        </i>

        <i>
         filho mais novo
        </i>
        ), and
        <i>
         moleque
        </i>
        ("brat, spoiled child").
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Although the African slaves had various ethnic origins, by far most of the borrowings were contributed (1) by
       <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">
        Bantu languages
       </a>
       (above all,
       <a href="/wiki/Kimbundu" title="Kimbundu">
        Kimbundu
       </a>
       , from
       <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">
        Angola
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kikongo" title="Kikongo">
        Kikongo
       </a>
       from Angola and the area that is now the
       <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Republic of the Congo">
        Republic of the Congo
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">
        Democratic Republic of the Congo
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and (2) by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niger-Congo_languages" title="Niger-Congo languages">
        Niger-Congo languages
       </a>
       , notably
       <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_language" title="Yoruba language">
        Yoruba
       </a>
       /Nagô, from what is now
       <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">
        Nigeria
       </a>
       , and Jeje/
       <a href="/wiki/Ewe_language" title="Ewe language">
        Ewe
       </a>
       , from what is now
       <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">
        Benin
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There are also many loanwords from other European languages, including
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English,
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       . In addition, there is a limited set of vocabulary from
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">
        Japanese
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Portuguese has borrowed a large number of words from English. In Brazil, these are especially related to the following fields (note that some of these words are used in other Portuguese-speaking countries):
      </p>

         pub
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         bartender
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         barman
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         lanche
        </i>
        ("portable lunch"),
        <i>
         underground
        </i>
        (cultural),
        <i>
         flop
        </i>
        (movie/TV context and slang),
        <i>
         DJ
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         VJ
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Haole" title="Haole">
          haole
         </a>
        </i>
        (slang, brought from Hawaii by surfers).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Many of these words are used throughout the
       <a href="/wiki/Lusophone" title="Lusophone">
        Lusosphere
       </a>
       .
      </p>

       </i>

       </i>

       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        haras
       </i>

       <i>
        calandragem
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cabaré
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        coqueluche
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        coquine
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        coquette
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        cocotinha
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        galã
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        bas-fond
       </i>
       (used as slang),
       <i>
        mascote
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        estampa
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sabotagem
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        RSVP
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        rendez-vous
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        chez...
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        à la carte
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        à la ...
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        forró, forrobodó
       </i>
       (from 19th-century
       <i>
        faux-bourdon
       </i>
       ). Brazilian Portuguese tends to adopt French suffixes as in
       <i>
        aterrissagem
       </i>
       (Fr.
       <i>
        atterrissage
       </i>
       "landing [aviation]"), differently from European Portuguese (cf. Eur.Port.
       <i>
        aterragem
       </i>
       ). Brazilian Portuguese (BP) also tends to adopt culture-bound concepts from French. That is the difference between BP
       <i>
        estação
       </i>
       ("station") and EP
       <i>
        gare
       </i>
       ("train station"—Portugal also uses
       <i>
        estação
       </i>
       ). BP
       <i>
        trem
       </i>
       is from English
       <i>
        train
       </i>
       (ultimately from French), while EP
       <i>
        comboio
       </i>
       is from Fr.
       <i>
        convoi
       </i>
       . An evident example of the dichotomy between English and French influences can be noted in the use of the expressions
       <i>
        know-how
       </i>
       , used in a technical context, and
       <i>
        savoir-faire
       </i>
       in a social context. Portugal uses the expression
       <i>
        hora de ponta
       </i>
       , from French
       <i>
        l'heure de pointe
       </i>
       , to refer to the "rush hour", while Brazil has
       <i>
        horário de pico, horário de pique
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        hora do rush
       </i>
       . Both
       <i>
        bilhar
       </i>
       , from French
       <i>
        billard
       </i>
       , and the phonetic adaptation
       <i>
        sinuca
       </i>
       are used interchangeably for "snooker".
      </p>
      <p>
       Contributions from
       <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">
        German
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       include terms for foods, music, the arts, and architecture.
      </p>
      <p>
       From German, besides
       <a href="/wiki/Strudel" title="Strudel">
        strudel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pretzel" title="Pretzel">
        pretzel
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bratwurst" title="Bratwurst">
        bratwurst
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Kuchen" title="Kuchen">
        kuchen
       </a>
       (also
       <i>
        bolo cuca
       </i>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Sauerkraut" title="Sauerkraut">
        sauerkraut
       </a>
       (also spelled
       <i>
        chucrute
       </i>
       from French
       <i>
        choucrout
       </i>
       and pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃuˈkɾutʃi]
       </span>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/Wurstsalat" title="Wurstsalat">
        wurstsalat
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sauerbraten" title="Sauerbraten">
        sauerbraten
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oktoberfest" title="Oktoberfest">
        Oktoberfest
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biergarten" title="Biergarten">
        biergarten
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        zelt
       </i>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Osterbaum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Osterbaum (page does not exist)">
        Osterbaum
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bauernfest" title="Bauernfest">
        Bauernfest
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sch%C3%BCtzenfest" title="Schützenfest">
        Schützenfest
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        hinterland
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Kindergarten
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        bock
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        fassbier
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        chope
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        Schoppen
       </i>
       ), there are also abstract terms from German such as
       <i>
        Prost
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        zum wohl
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        doppelgänger
       </i>
       (also
       <i>
        sósia
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        über
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        brinde
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kitsch
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ersatz
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        blitz
       </i>
       ("police action"), and possibly
       <i>
        encrenca
       </i>
       ("difficult situation", perhaps from Ger.
       <i>
        ein Kranker
       </i>
       , "a sick person").
       <i>
        Xumbergar
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        brega
       </i>
       (from marshal
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Schomberg,_1st_Duke_of_Schomberg" title="Frederick Schomberg, 1st Duke of Schomberg">
        Friedrich Hermann Von Schönberg
       </a>
       ), and
       <i>
        xote
       </i>
       (musical style and dance) from
       <i>
        schottisch
       </i>
       . A significant number of beer brands in Brazil are named after German culture-bound concepts and place names due the fact that the brewing process was brought by German immigrants.
      </p>
      <p>
       Italian loan words and expressions, in addition to those that are related to food or music, include
       <i>
        tchau
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        "ciao"
       </i>

       <i>

       <i>
        ecco
       </i>

       <i>
        ecco fatto
       </i>

       <i>
        ecco qui
       </i>

       <i>

       </i>

       <i>
        schifoso
       </i>

       <i>

       </i>

       <i>

       </i>

       <i>

       </i>

       <i>

       </i>

       <i>

       <i>

       <i>

       </i>
       ,
       <i>

       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        grappa
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        stratoria
       </i>
       . Terms of endearment of Italian origin include
       <i>
        amore
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        bambino/a
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ragazzo/a
       </i>

       <i>
        caro/a mio/a
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tesoro
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        bello/a
       </i>
       ; also
       <i>
        babo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        mamma
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        baderna
       </i>
       (from
       <i>
        Marietta Baderna
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        carcamano
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        torcicolo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        casanova
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        noccia
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        noja
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        che me ne frega
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Io ti voglio tanto bene
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        ti voglio bene assai
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Fewer words have been borrowed from
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">
        Japanese
       </a>
       . The latter borrowings are also mostly related to food and drink or culture-bound concepts, such as
       <i>
        quimono
       </i>
       , from Japanese
       <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">
        kimono
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">
        <i>
         karaokê
        </i>
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        yakisoba
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        temakeria
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sushi bar
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        mangá
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        biombo
       </i>
       (from Portugal) (from
       <i>
        byó bu sukurín
       </i>
       , "folding screen"),
       <i>
        jó ken pô
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        jankenpon
       </i>
       ("
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rock-paper-scissors" title="Rock-paper-scissors">
        rock-paper-scissors
       </a>
       ", played with the Japanese words being said before the start),
       <i>
        saquê
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sashimi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tempurá
       </i>
       (a lexical "loan repayment" from a Portuguese loanword in Japanese),
       <i>
        hashi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        wasabi
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        johrei
       </i>
       (religious philosophy),
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_diaspora" title="Japanese diaspora">
        <i>
         nikkei
        </i>
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        gaijin
       </i>
       ("non-Japanese"),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Issei" title="Issei">
         issei
        </a>
       </i>
       ("Japanese immigrant")—as well as the different descending generations
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nisei" title="Nisei">
         nisei
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sansei" title="Sansei">
         sansei
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Yonsei_(Japanese_diaspora)" title="Yonsei (Japanese diaspora)">
         yonsei
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        gossei
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        rokussei
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        shichissei
       </i>
       . Other Japanese loanwords include racial terms, such as
       <i>
        ainoko
       </i>
       ("Eurasian") and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hafu" title="Hafu">
         hafu
        </a>
       </i>
       (from English
       <i>
        half
       </i>
       ); work-related, socioeconomic, historical, and ethnic terms limited to some spheres of society, including
       <i>
        koseki
       </i>
       ("genealogical research"),
       <i>
        dekassegui
       </i>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Dekasegi" title="Dekasegi">
        dekasegi
       </a>
       "),
       <i>
        arubaito
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kaizen
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        seiketsu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        karoshi
       </i>
       ("death by work excess"),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Burakumin" title="Burakumin">
         burakumin
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kamikaze
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        seppuku
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        harakiri
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        jisatsu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        jigai
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        ainu
       </i>
       ; martial arts terms such as
       <i>
        karatê
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        aikidô
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        bushidô
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        katana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        judô
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        jiu-jítsu
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kyudô
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Nunchaku" title="Nunchaku">
         nunchaku
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        sumô
       </i>
       ; terms related to writing, such as
       <i>
        kanji
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        katakana
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        hiragana
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        romaji
       </i>
       ; and terms for art concepts such as
       <i>
        kabuki
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ikebana
       </i>
       . Other culture-bound terms from Japanese include
       <a href="/wiki/Furo" title="Furo">
        <i>
         ofurô
        </i>
       </a>
       ("Japanese bathtub"),
       <i>
        Nihong
       </i>
       ("target news niche and websites"),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Kabocha" title="Kabocha">
         kabocha
        </a>
       </i>
       (type of pumpkin introduced in Japan by the Portuguese),
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Reiki" title="Reiki">
         reiki
        </a>
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Shiatsu" title="Shiatsu">
         shiatsu
        </a>
       </i>
       . Some words have popular usage while others are known for a specific context in specific circles. Terms used among
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_diaspora" title="Japanese diaspora">
        Nikkei
       </a>
       descendants include
       <i>
        oba-chan
       </i>
       ("grandma");
       <i>
        onee-san
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        onee-chan
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        onii-san
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        onii-chan
       </i>
       ; toasts and salutations such as
       <i>
        kampai
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        banzai
       </i>
       ; and some
       <a href="/wiki/Honorific_speech_in_Japanese" title="Honorific speech in Japanese">
        honorific
       </a>
       suffixes of address such as
       <i>
        chan
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kun
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        sama
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        san, and
       </i>
       senpai
       <i>
        .
       </i>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">
        Chinese
       </a>
       contributed a few terms such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Tai_chi" title="Tai chi">
         tai chi chuan
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        chá
       </i>
       ("tea")—also in European Portuguese.
      </p>
      <p>
       The loan vocabulary includes several
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calques" title="Calques">
        calques
       </a>
       , such as
       <i>
        arranha-céu
       </i>
       ("skyscraper", from French
       <i>
        gratte-ciel
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        cachorro-quente
       </i>
       (from English
       <i>
        hot dog
       </i>
       ) in Portuguese worldwide.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_influences">
        Other influences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Other influences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Use of the reflexive
       <i>
        me
       </i>
       , especially in São Paulo and
       <a href="/wiki/South_Region,_Brazil" title="South Region, Brazil">
        the South
       </a>
       , is thought to be an Italianism, attributed to the large Italian immigrant population, as are certain prosodic features, including patterns of intonation and stress, also in the South and
       <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Southeast Region, Brazil">
        Southeast
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Some authors claim that the loss of initial
       <i>
        es-
       </i>
       in the forms of the verb
       <i>
        estar
       </i>
       (e.g. "Tá bom") —now widespread in Brazil —reflects an influence from the speech of African slaves.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">
          <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (April 2015)">
           dubious
          </span>
         </a>
         <span class="metadata">
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Brazilian_Portuguese#Dubious" title="Talk:Brazilian Portuguese">
           discuss
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The same feature, however, can be found in European Portuguese and several other Romance languages. It is also claimed that some common grammatical features of Brazilian Portuguese —such as the near-complete disappearance of certain verb inflections and a marked preference for the
       <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">
        periphrastic future
       </a>
       (e.g. "vou falar") over the synthetic future ("falarei") —recall the grammatical simplification typical of
       <a href="/wiki/Pidgin" title="Pidgin">
        pidgins
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Creole_language" title="Creole language">
        creoles
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute">
          <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (April 2015)">
           dubious
          </span>
         </a>
         <span class="metadata">
          –
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Brazilian_Portuguese#Dubious" title="Talk:Brazilian Portuguese">
           discuss
          </a>
         </span>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Other scholars, however—notably Naro &amp; Scherre
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naro2007_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naro2007-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       —have noted that the same or similar processes can be observed in the European variant, as well as in many varieties of Spanish, and that the main features of Brazilian Portuguese can be traced directly from 16th-century European Portuguese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naro2007_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naro2007-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, they find many of the same phenomena in other Romance languages, including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aranese" title="Aranese">
        Aranese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">
        French
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">
        Romanian
       </a>
       ; they explain these phenomena as due to natural Romance
       <a href="/wiki/Drift_(linguistics)" title="Drift (linguistics)">
        drift
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naro2007_10-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naro2007-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Naro and Scherre affirm that Brazilian Portuguese is not a "decreolized" form, but rather the "
       <a href="/wiki/Nativization" title="Nativization">
        nativization
       </a>
       " of a "radical Romanic" form.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naro2007_10-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naro2007-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They assert that the phenomena found in Brazilian Portuguese are inherited from Classical Latin and Old Portuguese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naro2007_10-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Naro2007-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to another linguist,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       vernacular Brazilian Portuguese is continuous with European Portuguese, while its phonetics is more conservative in several aspects, characterizing the nativization of a
       <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">
        koiné
       </a>
       formed by several regional European Portuguese varieties brought to Brazil, modified by natural drift.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Written_and_spoken_languages">
        Written and spoken languages
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Written and spoken languages">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The written language taught in Brazilian schools has historically been based on the standard of Portugal by law enforcement, and until the 19th century, Portuguese writers have often been regarded as models by some Brazilian authors and university professors. Nonetheless, this closeness and aspiration to unity was severely weakened in the 20th century by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Week_of_Modern_Art" title="Week of Modern Art">
        nationalist movements in literature and the arts
       </a>
       , which awakened in many Brazilians the desire for true (own) national writing uninfluenced by standards in Portugal. Later on, agreements were made as to preserve at least the orthographical unity throughout the Portuguese-speaking world, including the African and Asian variants of the language (which are typically more similar to EP, due to a Portuguese presence lasting into the end of the 20th century).
      </p>
      <p>
       On the other hand, the spoken language suffered none of the constraints that applied to the written language and Brazilian Portuguese sounds very different than any other derivative of Portuguese. Brazilians, when concerned with pronunciation, look up to what is considered the national standard variety, and never the European one, specially after a lot of tension between Portugal and the settlers (immigrants) in Brazil since the country's de-facto settlement, as immigrants were forbidden of speaking freely on their native languages in Brazil by fear of severe punishment by the Portuguese law enforcers. Lately, Brazilians in general have had some exposure to European speech, through TV and music. Often one will see Brazilian actors working in Portugal, and Portuguese actors working in Brazil, despite the differences on spoken language.
      </p>
      <p>
       Regardless, modern Brazilian Portuguese was highly influenced by other languages introduced by immigrants through the past century, specifically by German, Italian and Japanese immigrants. This high intake of immigrants not only caused the incorporation and/or adaptation of many words and expressions from their native language into local language but also created specific dialects as it is the case of German's
       <i>
        Hunsrückisch
       </i>
       dialect in the South of Brazil. This is also one of the main reasons why Brazilian Portuguese sounds so different from other Portuguese derivatives.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Formal_writing">
        Formal writing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Formal writing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The written Brazilian standard differs from the European one to about the same extent that written
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_and_British_English_differences" title="American and British English differences">
        American English differs from written British English
       </a>
       . The differences extend to spelling, lexicon, and grammar. However, with the entry into force of the Orthographic Agreement of 1990 in Portugal and in Brazil since 2009, these differences were drastically reduced.
      </p>
      <p>
       Several Brazilian writers have been awarded with the highest prize of the Portuguese language. The
       <a href="/wiki/Cam%C3%B5es_Prize" title="Camões Prize">
        Camões Prize
       </a>
       awarded annually by Portuguese and Brazilians is often regarded as the equivalent of the Nobel Prize in Literature for works in Portuguese.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joaquim_Maria_Machado_de_Assis" title="Joaquim Maria Machado de Assis">
        Joaquim Maria Machado de Assis
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Guimar%C3%A3es_Rosa" title="João Guimarães Rosa">
        João Guimarães Rosa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Drummond_de_Andrade" title="Carlos Drummond de Andrade">
        Carlos Drummond de Andrade
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Graciliano_Ramos" title="Graciliano Ramos">
        Graciliano Ramos
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Cabral_de_Melo_Neto" title="João Cabral de Melo Neto">
        João Cabral de Melo Neto
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Cec%C3%ADlia_Meireles" title="Cecília Meireles">
        Cecília Meireles
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Clarice_Lispector" title="Clarice Lispector">
        Clarice Lispector
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_de_Alencar" title="José de Alencar">
        José de Alencar
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rachel_de_Queiroz" title="Rachel de Queiroz">
        Rachel de Queiroz
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Amado" title="Jorge Amado">
        Jorge Amado
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Castro_Alves" title="Castro Alves">
        Castro Alves
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Candido" title="Antonio Candido">
        Antonio Candido
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Autran_Dourado" title="Autran Dourado">
        Autran Dourado
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Rubem_Fonseca" title="Rubem Fonseca">
        Rubem Fonseca
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lygia_Fagundes_Telles" title="Lygia Fagundes Telles">
        Lygia Fagundes Telles
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Euclides_da_Cunha" title="Euclides da Cunha">
        Euclides da Cunha
       </a>
       are Brazilian writers recognized for writing the most outstanding work in the Portuguese language.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spelling_differences">
        Spelling differences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Spelling differences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Reforms_of_Portuguese_orthography" title="Reforms of Portuguese orthography">
        Reforms of Portuguese orthography
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Brazilian spellings of certain words differ from those used in Portugal and the other Portuguese-speaking countries. Some of these differences are merely orthographic, but others reflect true differences in pronunciation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Until the implementation of the 1990 orthographic reform, a major subset of the differences related to the consonant clusters
       <i>
        cc
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        cç
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ct
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        pc
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        pç
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        pt
       </i>
       . In many cases, the letters
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        p
       </i>
       in syllable-final position have become silent in all varieties of Portuguese, a common phonetic change in Romance languages (cf. Spanish
       <i>
        objeto
       </i>
       , French
       <i>
        objet
       </i>
       ). Accordingly, they stopped being written in BP (compare
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">
        Italian
       </a>
       spelling standards), but continued to be written in other Portuguese-speaking countries. For example, we had EP
       <i>
        acção
       </i>
       / BP
       <i>
        ação
       </i>
       ("action"), EP
       <i>
        óptimo
       </i>
       / BP
       <i>
        ótimo
       </i>
       ("optimum"), and so on, where the consonant was silent both in BP and EP, but the words were spelled differently. Only in a small number of words is the consonant silent in Brazil and pronounced elsewhere or vice versa, as in the case of BP
       <i>
        fato
       </i>
       , but EP
       <i>
        facto
       </i>
       . However, the new Portuguese language orthographic reform led to the elimination of the writing of the silent consonants also in the EP, making now the writing system virtually identical in all of the Portuguese-speaking countries,
      </p>
      <p>
       However, BP has retained those
       <a href="/wiki/Silent_letter" title="Silent letter">
        silent consonants
       </a>
       in a few cases, such as
       <i>
        detectar
       </i>
       ("to detect"). In particular, BP generally distinguishes in sound and writing between
       <i>
        secção
       </i>
       ("section" as in
       <i>
        anatomy
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        drafting
       </i>
       ) and
       <i>
        seção
       </i>
       ("section" of an organization); whereas EP uses
       <i>
        secção
       </i>
       for both senses.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another major set of differences is the BP usage of
       <i>
        ô
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        ê
       </i>
       in many words where EP has
       <i>
        ó
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        é
       </i>
       , such as BP
       <i>
        neurônio
       </i>
       / EP
       <i>
        neurónio
       </i>
       ("neuron") and BP
       <i>
        arsênico
       </i>
       / EP
       <i>
        arsénico
       </i>
       ("arsenic"). These spelling differences are due to genuinely different pronunciations. In EP, the vowels
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       may be open (
       <i>
        é
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        ó
       </i>
       ) or closed (
       <i>
        ê
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        ô
       </i>
       ) when they are stressed before one of the nasal consonants
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       followed by a vowel, but in BP they are always closed in this environment. The variant spellings are necessary in those cases because the general Portuguese spelling rules mandate a stress diacritic in those words, and the Portuguese diacritics also encode vowel quality.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another source of variation is the spelling of the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʒ]
       </span>
       sound before
       <i>
        e
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       . By Portuguese spelling rules, that sound can be written either as
       <i>
        j
       </i>
       (favored in BP for certain words) or
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       (favored in EP). Thus, for example, we have BP
       <i>
        berinjela
       </i>
       / EP
       <i>
        beringela
       </i>
       ("eggplant").
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_Register_-_Formal_vs._Informal">
        Language Register - Formal vs. Informal
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Language Register - Formal vs. Informal">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The linguistic situation of the BP informal speech in relation to the standard language is controversial. There are authors (Bortoni, Kato, Mattos e Silva, Bagno, Perini) who describe it as a case of
       <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
        diglossia
       </a>
       , considering that informal BP has developed – both in
       <a href="/wiki/Phonetics" title="Phonetics">
        phonetics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">
        grammar
       </a>
       – in its own particular way.
      </p>
      <p>
       Accordingly, the formal register of Brazilian Portuguese has a written and spoken form. The written formal register (FW) is used in almost all printed media and written communication, is uniform throughout the country and is the "Portuguese" officially taught at school. The spoken formal register (FS) is essentially a phonetic rendering of the written form. (FS) is used in very formal situations, such as speeches or ceremonies or when reading directly out of a text. While (FS) is necessarily uniform in lexicon and grammar, it shows noticeable regional variations in pronunciation.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics_of_Informal_Brazilian_Portuguese">
        Characteristics of Informal Brazilian Portuguese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Characteristics of Informal Brazilian Portuguese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The main and most general (i.e. not considering various regional variations) characteristics of the informal variant of BP are the following. To be noted that these characteristics are also present in other varieties of Portuguese:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        dropping the first syllable of the verb
        <i>
         estar
        </i>
        ("[statal/incidental] to be") throughout the conjugation (
        <i>
         ele tá
        </i>
        ("he's") instead of
        <i>
         ele está
        </i>
        ("he is"),
        <i>
         nós táva(mos/mo)
        </i>
        ("we were") instead of
        <i>
         nós estávamos
        </i>
        ("we were"));
       </li>
       <li>
        dropping prepositions before subordinate and relative clauses beginning with conjunctions (
        <i>
         Ele precisa que vocês ajudem
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         Ele precisa de que vocês ajudem
        </i>
        );
       </li>
       <li>
        replacing
        <i>
         haver
        </i>
        when it means "to exist" with
        <i>
         ter
        </i>
        ("to have"):
        <i>
         há muitos problemas na cidade
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
         <a href="#cite_note-16">
          [16]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ("there are many problems in the city") can be heard, but is much rarer than
        <i>
         tem muitos problema(s) na cidade.
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
         <a href="#cite_note-17">
          [17]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        lack of third-person object pronouns, which may be omitted completely or replaced by their respective personal pronouns (
        <i>
         eu vi ele
        </i>
        or even just
        <i>
         eu vi
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         eu o vi
        </i>
        for "I saw him/it")
       </li>
       <li>
        lack of second-person verb forms (except for some parts of Brazil) and, in various regions, plural third-person forms as well (
        <i>
         tu cantas
        </i>
        becomes
        <i>
         tu canta
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         você canta
        </i>
        (Brazilian uses the pronoun "você" a lot but rarely uses "tu", except in some states such as Amazonas and Rio Grande do Sul, in the latter "você" is almost never used in informal speech, with "tu" being used instead, using both second and third-person forms depending on the speaker)
       </li>
       <li>
        lack of the relative pronoun
        <i>
         cujo/cuja
        </i>
        ("whose"), which is replaced by
        <i>
         que
        </i>
        ("that/which"), either alone (the possession being implied) or along with a possessive pronoun or expression, such as
        <i>
         dele/dela
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         A mulher cujo filho morreu
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
         <a href="#cite_note-18">
          [18]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ("the woman whose son died") becomes
        <i>
         A mulher que o filho [dela] morreu
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
         <a href="#cite_note-19">
          [19]
         </a>
        </sup>
        ("the woman that [her] son died"))
       </li>
       <li>
        frequent use of the pronoun
        <i>
         a gente
        </i>
        ("people") with 3rd p. sg verb forms instead of the 1st p. pl verb forms and pronoun
        <i>
         nós
        </i>
        ("we/us"), though both are formally correct and
        <i>
         nós
        </i>
        is still much used.
       </li>
       <li>
        obligatory
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/proclisis" title="wikt:proclisis">
         proclisis
        </a>
        in all cases (always
        <i>
         me disseram
        </i>
        , rarely
        <i>
         disseram-me
        </i>
        ), as well as use of the pronoun between two verbs in a verbal expression (always
        <i>
         vem me treinando
        </i>
        , never
        <i>
         me vem treinando
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         vem treinando-me
        </i>
        )
       </li>
       <li>
        contracting certain high-frequency phrases, which is not necessarily unacceptable in standard BP (
        <i>
         para
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         pra
        </i>
        ;
        <i>
         dependo de ele ajudar
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         dependo 'dele' ajudar
        </i>
        ;
        <i>
         com as
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         c'as
        </i>
        ;
        <i>
         deixa eu ver
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         x'o vê
        </i>
        ;
        <i>
         você está
        </i>
        &gt;
        <i>
         cê tá
        </i>
        etc.)
       </li>
       <li>
        preference for
        <i>
         para
        </i>
        over
        <i>
         a
        </i>
        in the directional meaning (
        <i>
         Para onde você vai?
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         Aonde você vai?
        </i>
        ("Where are you going?"))
       </li>
       <li>
        use of certain idiomatic expressions, such as
        <i>
         Cadê o carro?
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         Onde está o carro?
        </i>
        ("Where is the car?")
       </li>
       <li>
        lack of indirect object pronouns, especially
        <i>
         lhe
        </i>
        , which are replaced by
        <i>
         para
        </i>
        plus their respective personal pronoun (
        <i>
         Dê um copo de água para ele
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         Dê-lhe um copo de água
        </i>
        ("Give him a glass of water");
        <i>
         Quero mandar uma carta para você
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         Quero lhe mandar uma carta
        </i>
        ("I want to send you a letter"))
       </li>
       <li>
        use of
        <i>
         aí
        </i>
        as a pronoun for indefinite direct objects (similar to
        <a href="/wiki/French_personal_pronouns#The_pronoun_en" title="French personal pronouns">
         French 'en'
        </a>
        ). Examples:
        <i>
         falaí (fala + aí)
        </i>
        ("say it"),
        <i>
         esconde aí
        </i>
        ("hide it"),
        <i>
         pera aí
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         espera aí
        </i>
        = "wait a moment");
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Impersonal_verb" title="Impersonal verb">
         impersonal
        </a>
        use of the verb
        <i>
         dar
        </i>
        ("to give") to express that something is feasible or permissible. Example:
        <i>
         dá pra eu comer?
        </i>
        ("can/may I eat it?");
        <i>
         deu pra eu entender
        </i>
        ("I could understand");
        <i>
         dá pra ver um homem na foto
        </i>
        instead of
        <i>
         pode-se ver um homem na foto
        </i>
        ("it's possible to see a man in the picture")
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntactic_and_morphological_features">
        Syntactic and morphological features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Syntactic and morphological features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Topic-prominent_language">
        Topic-prominent language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Topic-prominent language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Modern
       <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">
        linguistic
       </a>
       studies have shown that Brazilian Portuguese is a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Topic_(linguistics)" title="Topic (linguistics)">
        topic
       </a>
       -prominent or topic- and subject-prominent language.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sentences with topic are extensively used in Portuguese, perhaps more in Brazilian Portuguese most often by means of turning an element (object or verb) in the sentence into an introductory phrase, on which the body of the sentence constitutes a comment (topicalization), thus emphasizing it, as in
       <i>
        Esses assuntos eu não conheço bem
       </i>
       – literally, "These subjects I don't know [them] well".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although this sentence would be perfectly acceptable in Portugal. In fact, in the Portuguese language, the anticipation of the verb or object at the beginning of the sentence, repeating it or using the respective pronoun referring to it, is also quite common, e.g. in
       <i>
        Essa menina, eu não sei o que fazer com ela
       </i>
       ("This girl, I don't know what to do with her") or
       <i>
        Com essa menina eu não sei o que fazer
       </i>
       ("With this girl I don't know what to do").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The use of redundant pronouns for means of topicalization is considered grammatically incorrect, because the topicalized noun phrase, according to traditional European analysis, has no syntactic function. This kind of construction, however, is often used in European Portuguese. Brazilian grammars traditionally treat this structure similarly, rarely mentioning such a thing as
       <i>
        topic
       </i>
       . Nevertheless, the so-called
       <a href="/wiki/Anacoluthon" title="Anacoluthon">
        anacoluthon
       </a>
       has taken on a new dimension in Brazilian Portuguese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The poet
       <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Drummond_de_Andrade" title="Carlos Drummond de Andrade">
        Carlos Drummond de Andrade
       </a>
       once wrote a short
       <i>
        metapoema
       </i>
       (a
       <i>
        metapoem
       </i>
       , i. e., a poem about poetry, a specialty for which he was renowned) treating the concept of
       <i>
        anacoluto
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <blockquote class="templatequote">
       <p>
        [...] O homem, chamar-lhe mito não passa de anacoluto
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
         <a href="#cite_note-24">
          [24]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (The man, calling him myth is nothing more than an anacoluthon).
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In colloquial language, this kind of
       <i>
        anacoluto
       </i>
       may even be used when the subject itself is the topic, only to add more emphasis to this fact, e.g. the sentence
       <i>
        Essa menina, ela costuma tomar conta de cachorros abandonados
       </i>
       ("This girl, she usually takes care of abandoned dogs"). This structure highlights the topic, and could be more accurately translated as "As for this girl, she usually takes care of abandoned dogs".
      </p>
      <p>
       The use of this construction is particularly common with
       <a href="/wiki/Compound_subject" title="Compound subject">
        compound subjects
       </a>
       , as in, e.g.,
       <i>
        Eu e ela, nós fomos passear
       </i>
       ("She and I, we went for a walk"). This happens because the traditional syntax (
       <i>
        Eu e ela fomos passear
       </i>
       ) places a plural-conjugated verb immediately following an argument in the singular, which may sound unnatural to Brazilian ears. The redundant pronoun thus clarifies the verbal inflection in such cases.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Progressive">
        Progressive
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Progressive">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Portuguese makes extensive use of verbs in the progressive aspect, almost as in English.
      </p>
      <p>
       Brazilian Portuguese seldom has the present continuous construct
       <b>
        <i>
         estar a
        </i>
        + infinitive
       </b>
       , which, in contrast, has become quite common in European over the last few centuries. BP maintains the Classical Portuguese form of continuous expression, which is made by
       <b>
        <i>
         estar
        </i>
        +
        <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">
         gerund
        </a>
       </b>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Thus, Brazilians will always write
       <i>
        ela está dançando
       </i>
       ("she is dancing"), not
       <i>
        ela está a dançar
       </i>
       . The same restriction applies to several other uses of the gerund: BP uses
       <i>
        ficamos conversando
       </i>
       ("we kept on talking") and
       <i>
        ele trabalha cantando
       </i>
       ("he sings while he works"), but rarely
       <i>
        ficamos a conversar
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ele trabalha a cantar
       </i>
       as is the case in most varieties of EP.
      </p>
      <p>
       BP retains the combination
       <i>
        a
       </i>
       + infinitive for uses that are not related to continued action, such as
       <i>
        voltamos a correr
       </i>
       ("we went back to running"). Some dialects of EP [namely from
       <a href="/wiki/Alentejo" title="Alentejo">
        Alentejo
       </a>
       , Algarve, Açores (Azores), and Madeira] also tend to feature
       <i>
        estar
       </i>
       +
       <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">
        gerund
       </a>
       , as in Brazil.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_pronouns">
        Personal pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Personal pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_personal_pronouns" title="Portuguese personal pronouns">
        Portuguese personal pronouns
       </a>
      </div>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax">
        Syntax
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Syntax">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In general, the dialects that gave birth to Portuguese had a quite flexible use of the object pronouns in the proclitic or enclitic positions. In Classical Portuguese, the use of proclisis was very extensive, while, on the contrary, in modern European Portuguese the use of enclisis has become indisputably majoritary.
      </p>
      <p>
       Brazilians normally place the
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        object pronoun
       </a>
       before the verb (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proclitic" title="Proclitic">
        proclitic
       </a>
       position), as in
       <i>
        ele me viu
       </i>
       ("he saw me"). In many such cases, the proclisis would be considered awkward or even grammatically incorrect in EP, in which the pronoun is generally placed after the verb (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enclitic" title="Enclitic">
        enclitic
       </a>
       position), namely
       <i>
        ele viu-me
       </i>
       . However, formal BP still follows EP in avoiding starting a sentence with a proclitic pronoun; so both will write
       <i>
        Deram-lhe o livro
       </i>
       ("They gave him/her the book") instead of
       <i>
        Lhe deram o livro
       </i>
       , though it will seldom be spoken in BP (but would be clearly understood).
      </p>
      <p>
       However, in verb expressions accompanied by an object pronoun, Brazilians normally place it amid the auxiliary verb and the main one (
       <i>
        ela vem me pagando
       </i>
       but not
       <i>
        ela me vem pagando
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        ela vem pagando-me
       </i>
       ). In some cases, in order to adapt this use to the standard grammar, some Brazilian scholars recommend that
       <i>
        ela vem me pagando
       </i>
       should be written like
       <i>
        ela vem-me pagando
       </i>
       (as in EP), in which case the enclisis could be totally acceptable if there would not be a factor of proclisis. Therefore, this phenomenon may or not be considered improper according to the prescribed grammar, since, according to the case, there could be a factor of proclisis that would not permit the placement of the pronoun between the verbs (e.g. when there is a negative adverb near the pronoun, in which case the standard grammar prescribes proclisis,
       <i>
        ela não me vem pagando
       </i>
       and not
       <i>
        ela não vem-me pagando
       </i>
       ). Nevertheless, nowadays, it is becoming perfectly acceptable to use a clitic between two verbs, without linking it with a hyphen (as in
       <i>
        poderia se dizer
       </i>
       , or
       <i>
        não vamos lhes dizer
       </i>
       ) and this usage (known as:
       <i>
        pronome solto entre dois verbos
       </i>
       ) can be found in modern(ist) literature, textbooks, magazines and newspapers like
       <a href="/wiki/Folha_de_S.Paulo" title="Folha de S.Paulo">
        Folha de S.Paulo
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/O_Estado_de_S._Paulo" title="O Estado de S. Paulo">
        O Estadão
       </a>
       (see in-house style manuals of these newspapers, available on-line, for more details).
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Contracted_forms">
        Contracted forms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Contracted forms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       BP rarely uses the contracted combinations of direct and indirect object pronouns which are sometimes used in EP, such as
       <i>
        me
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        o
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        mo
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        lhe
       </i>
       +
       <i>
        as
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        lhas
       </i>
       . Instead, the indirect clitic is replaced by preposition + strong pronoun: thus BP writes
       <i>
        ela o deu para mim
       </i>
       ("she gave it to me") instead of EP
       <i>
        ela deu-mo
       </i>
       ; the latter most probably will not be understood by Brazilians, being obsolete in BP.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mesoclisis">
        Mesoclisis
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Mesoclisis">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
        mesoclitic placement
       </a>
       of pronouns (between the verb stem and its inflection suffix) is viewed as archaic in BP, and therefore is restricted to very formal situations or stylistic texts. Hence the phrase
       <i>
        Eu dar-lhe-ia
       </i>
       , still current in EP, would be normally written
       <i>
        Eu lhe daria
       </i>
       in BP. Incidentally, a marked fondness for enclitic and mesoclitic pronouns was one of the many memorable eccentricities of former Brazilian President
       <a href="/wiki/J%C3%A2nio_Quadros" title="Jânio Quadros">
        Jânio Quadros
       </a>
       , as in his famous quote
       <i>
        Bebo-o porque é líquido, se fosse sólido comê-lo-ia
       </i>
       ("I drink it [liquor] because it is liquid, if it were solid I would eat it")
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Preferences">
        Preferences
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Preferences">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are many differences between formal written BP and EP that are simply a matter of different preferences between two alternative words or constructions that are both officially valid and acceptable.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_versus_compound_tenses">
        Simple versus compound tenses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Simple versus compound tenses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       A few synthetic tenses are usually replaced by compound tenses, such as in:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        future indicative:
        <i>
         eu cantarei
        </i>
        (simple),
        <i>
         eu vou cantar
        </i>
        (compound,
        <i>
         ir
        </i>
        + infinitive)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        conditional:
        <i>
         eu cantaria
        </i>
        (simple),
        <i>
         eu iria/ia cantar
        </i>
        (compound,
        <i>
         ir
        </i>
        + infinitive)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        past perfect:
        <i>
         eu cantara
        </i>
        (simple),
        <i>
         eu tinha cantado
        </i>
        (compound,
        <i>
         ter
        </i>
        + past participle)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Also, spoken BP usually uses the verb
       <i>
        ter
       </i>
       ("own", "have", sense of possession) and rarely
       <i>
        haver
       </i>
       ("have", sense of existence, or "there to be"), especially as an auxiliary (as it can be seen above) and as a verb of existence.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        written:
        <i>
         ele havia/tinha cantado
        </i>
        (he had sung)
       </dd>
       <dd>
