        spoken:
        <i>
         ele tinha cantado
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        written:
        <i>
         ele podia haver/ter dito
        </i>
        (he might have said)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        spoken:
        <i>
         ele podia ter dito
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       This phenomenon is also observed in Portugal.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_in_formal_spoken_language">
        Differences in formal spoken language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Differences in formal spoken language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In many ways, Brazilian Portuguese (BP) is
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_(language)" title="Conservative (language)">
        conservative
       </a>
       in its phonology. That also is true of
       <a href="/wiki/Angolan_Portuguese" title="Angolan Portuguese">
        Angolan Portuguese
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tomean_Portuguese" title="São Tomean Portuguese">
        São Tomean Portuguese
       </a>
       , and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_Portuguese" title="African Portuguese">
        African dialects
       </a>
       . Brazilian Portuguese has eight oral vowels, five nasal vowels, and several
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diphthongs" title="Diphthongs">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Triphthongs" title="Triphthongs">
        triphthongs
       </a>
       , some oral and some nasal.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">
        Vowels
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Vowels">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The reduction of vowels is one of the main phonetic characteristics of Portuguese generally, but in Brazilian Portuguese the intensity and frequency of that phenomenon varies significantly.
      </p>
      <p>
       Vowels in Brazilian Portuguese generally are pronounced more openly than in European Portuguese, even when reduced. In syllables that follow the stressed syllable, ⟨o⟩ is generally pronounced as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [u]
       </span>
       , ⟨a⟩ as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ]
       </span>
       , and ⟨e⟩ as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       . Some dialects of BP follow this pattern for vowels
       <i>
        before
       </i>
       the stressed syllable as well.
      </p>
      <p>
       In contrast, speakers of European Portuguese pronounce unstressed ⟨a⟩ primarily as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ]
       </span>
       , and they
       <a href="/wiki/Elision" title="Elision">
        elide
       </a>
       some unstressed vowels or reduce them to a short, near-close near-back unrounded vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [
        <a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">
         ɨ
        </a>
        ]
       </span>
       , a sound that does not exist in BP. Thus, for example, the word
       <i>
        setembro
       </i>
       is
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [seˈtẽbɾu]/[sɛˈtẽbɾu]
       </span>
       in BP, but
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s(ɨ)ˈtẽbɾu]
       </span>
       in European Portuguese.
      </p>
      <p>
       The main difference among the dialects of Brazilian Portuguese is the frequent presence or absence of open vowels in unstressed syllables. In dialects of the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Region,_Brazil" title="South Region, Brazil">
        South
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Southeast Region, Brazil">
        Southeast
       </a>
       , unstressed ⟨e⟩ and ⟨o⟩ (when they are not reduced to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [u]
       </span>
       ) are pronounced as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Close-mid_vowels" title="Close-mid vowels">
        close-mid vowels
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [e]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [o]
       </span>
       . Thus,
       <i>
        operação
       </i>
       (operation) and
       <i>
        rebolar
       </i>
       (to shake one's body) may be pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [opeɾaˈsɐ̃ũ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [heboˈla(h)]
       </span>
       . Open-mid vowels can occur only in the stressed syllable.
      </p>
      <p>
       Meanwhile, in accents of the
       <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">
        Northeast
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/North_Region,_Brazil" title="North Region, Brazil">
        North
       </a>
       , in patterns that have not yet been much studied, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open-mid_vowels" title="Open-mid vowels">
        open-mid vowels
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɛ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɔ]
       </span>
       can occur in unstressed syllables in a large number of words. Thus, the above examples would be pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɔpɛɾaˈsɐ̃ũ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [hɛbɔˈla(h)]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Another difference between Northern/Northeastern dialects and Southern/Southeastern ones is the pattern of
       <a href="/wiki/Nasalization" title="Nasalization">
        nasalization
       </a>
       of vowels before ⟨m⟩ and ⟨n⟩. In all dialects and all syllables, orthographic ⟨m⟩ or ⟨n⟩ followed by another consonant represents nasalization of the preceding vowel. But when the ⟨m⟩ or ⟨n⟩ is syllable-initial (i.e. followed by a vowel), it represents nasalization only of a preceding
       <i>
        stressed
       </i>
       vowel in the South and Southeast, as compared to nasalization of
       <i>
        any
       </i>
       vowel, regardless of stress, in the Northeast and North. A famous example of this distinction is the word
       <i>
        banana
       </i>
       , which a Northeasterner would pronounce
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [bɐ̃ˈnɐ̃nɐ]
       </span>
       , while a Southerner would pronounce
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [baˈnɐ̃nɐ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Vowel nasalization in some dialects of Brazilian Portuguese is very different from that of French, for example. In French, the nasalization extends uniformly through the entire vowel, whereas in the Southern-Southeastern dialects of Brazilian Portuguese, the nasalization begins almost imperceptibly and then becomes stronger toward the end of the vowel. In this respect it is more similar to the nasalization of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi-Urdu_phonology" title="Hindi-Urdu phonology">
        Hindi-Urdu phonology
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/Anusvara" title="Anusvara">
        Anusvara
       </a>
       ). In some cases, the nasal
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archiphoneme" title="Archiphoneme">
        archiphoneme
       </a>
       even entails the insertion of a
       <a href="/wiki/Nasal_consonant" title="Nasal consonant">
        nasal consonant
       </a>
       such as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [m, n, ŋ, ȷ̃, w̃, ɰ̃]
       </span>
       , as in the following examples:
      </p>
      <p>
       "manta" =
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈmɐ̃ntɐ/
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
       "tampa" =
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈtɐ̃mpɐ/
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
       "banco" =
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈbɐ̃ŋku/
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
       "bem" =
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /bẽȷ̃/
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
       "bom" =
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /bõʊ̃/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈbõɰ̃/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈbõŋ/
       </span>
      </p>
      <p>
       "pan" =
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈpɐ̃ɰ̃/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ˈpɐ̃ŋ/
       </span>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">
        Consonants
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Consonants">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <h5>
       <span id="Palatalization_of_.2Fdi.2F_and_.2Fti.2F">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Palatalization_of_/di/_and_/ti/">
        Palatalization of /di/ and /ti/
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Palatalization of /di/ and /ti/">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       One of the most noticeable tendencies of modern BP is the
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
        palatalization
       </a>
       of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       by most regions, which are pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʒ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃ]
       </span>
       (or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dᶾ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tᶴ]
       </span>
       ), respectively, before
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       . The word
       <i>
        presidente
       </i>
       "president", for example, is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pɾeziˈdẽtᶴi]
       </span>
       in these regions of Brazil but
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pɾɨziˈdẽt(ɨ)]
       </span>
       in Portugal. The pronunciation probably began in
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       and is often still associated with this city but is now standard in many other states and major cities, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Belo_Horizonte" title="Belo Horizonte">
        Belo Horizonte
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Salvador,_Bahia" title="Salvador, Bahia">
        Salvador
       </a>
       , and it has spread more recently to some regions of
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       (because of migrants from other regions), where it is common in most speakers under 40 or so. It has always been standard in Brazil's
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_people" title="Japanese people">
        Japanese
       </a>
       community since it is also a feature of
       <a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">
        Japanese
       </a>
       . The regions that still preserve the unpalatalized
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ti]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [di]
       </span>
       are mostly in the Northeast and South of
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       by the stronger influence from European Portuguese (Northeast), and from Italian and Argentine Spanish (South)
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="Attributing the more 'conservative' realization to Italian influence or neighbouring Spanish speaking communities is a mere (and very dubious) supposition, difficult to prove and with no widespread support in the linguistic community. (October 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       .
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Epenthesis_in_consonant_clusters">
        Epenthesis in consonant clusters
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Epenthesis in consonant clusters">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       BP tends to break up consonant clusters, if the second consonant is not
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /l/
       </span>
       , or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /s/
       </span>
       , by inserting an
       <a href="/wiki/Epenthesis" title="Epenthesis">
        epenthetic
       </a>
       vowel,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       , which can also be characterized, in some situations, as a
       <a href="/wiki/Schwa" title="Schwa">
        schwa
       </a>
       . The phenomenon happens mostly in the pretonic position and with the consonant clusters
       <i>
        ks
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ps
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        bj
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        dj
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        dv
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        kt
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        bt
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        ft
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        mn
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        tm
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        dm
       </i>
       : clusters that are not very common in the language ("afta":
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈaftɐ]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈafitɐ]
       </span>
       ; "opção" :
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɔpˈsɐ̃ũ]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɔpiˈsɐ̃ũ]
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       However, in some regions of Brazil (such as some Northeastern dialects), there has been an opposite tendency to reduce the unstressed vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [i]
       </span>
       into a very weak vowel so
       <i>
        partes
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        destratar
       </i>
       are often realized similarly to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [pahts]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [dʃtɾaˈta]
       </span>
       . Sometimes, the phenomenon occurs even more intensely in unstressed posttonic vowels (except the final ones) and causes the reduction of the word and the creation of new consonant clusters (
       <i>
        prática
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        prát'ca
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        máquina
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        maq'na
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        abóbora
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        abobra
       </i>
       ;
       <i>
        cócega
       </i>
       &gt;
       <i>
        cosca
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="L-vocalization_and_suppression_of_final_r">
        L-vocalization and suppression of final r
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: L-vocalization and suppression of final r">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Syllable-final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /l/
       </span>
       is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [u̯]
       </span>
       , and syllable-final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       is uvularized to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [χ]
       </span>
       or weakened to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [h]
       </span>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/North_Region,_Brazil" title="North Region, Brazil">
        North
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Region,_Brazil" title="Northeast Region, Brazil">
        Northeast
       </a>
       , while the state of
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/South_Region,_Brazil" title="South Region, Brazil">
        the South
       </a>
       conserve apical varieties of these phonemes. This—along with other adaptations—sometimes results in rather striking transformations of common
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loanwords" title="Loanwords">
        loanwords
       </a>
       . The brand name "McDonald's", for example, is rendered
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [mɛ̝kiˈdõnawdᶾis]
       </span>
       , and the word "rock" (the music) is rendered as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ˈhɔki]
       </span>
       . (Both initial
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       and doubled
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       are pronounced in BP as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [h]
       </span>
       , as is syllable-final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       .) Given that historical
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/
       </span>
       no longer appear in syllable-final position (having been replaced by nasalization of the preceding vowel), these varieties of BP have come to strongly favor open syllables.
      </p>
      <p>
       A related aspect of BP is the suppression of phrase-final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /r/
       </span>
       , even in formal speech. In most of Brazil, in formal situations, it may still be pronounced—as
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [χ]
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [h]
       </span>
       —at the end of a phrase. (Meanwhile, within a phrase where the following word begins with a vowel, it is pronounced as an apical flap:
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɾ]
       </span>
       .) Thus, verb infinitives like
       <i>
        matar
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        correr
       </i>
       in final position are normally pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [maˈta]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [koˈhe]
       </span>
       . (But compare "matar o tempo"
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [maˈta
        <b>
         ɾ
        </b>
        uˈtẽpu]
       </span>
       .) The same suppression also happens occasionally in EP, but much less often than in BP.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nasalization">
        Nasalization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Nasalization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Nasalization is much common in many BP dialects and is especially noticeable in vowels before
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/
       </span>
       before by a vowel. For the same reason, open vowels (which are not normally under nasalization in Portuguese) cannot occur before
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/
       </span>
       in BP, but can in EP. That sometimes affects the spelling of words. For example,
       <i>
        harmónico
       </i>
       "harmonic"
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐɾˈmɔniku]
       </span>
       is
       <i>
        harmônico
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [aɦˈmõniku]
       </span>
       in BP. It also can affect verbal paradigms: Portuguese distinguishes
       <i>
        falamos
       </i>
       "we speak"
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [fɐˈlɐ̃muʃ]
       </span>
       from 'falámos'
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [fɐˈlamuʃ]
       </span>
       "we spoke," but in BP, it is written and pronounced
       <i>
        falamos
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [faˈlɐ̃mus]
       </span>
       for both.
      </p>
      <p>
       Related is the difference in pronunciation of the consonant represented by
       <i>
        nh
       </i>
       in most BP dialects. It is always
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɲ]
       </span>
       in Portuguese, but in some regions of Brazil, it represents a nasalized semivowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j̃]
       </span>
       , which nasalizes the preceding vowel as well:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        manhãzinha
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [mɐ̃j̃ɐ̃zĩj̃ɐ]
       </span>
       ("early morning").
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonetic_changes">
        Phonetic changes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Phonetic changes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Several sound changes that affected Portuguese words were not shared by BP. Consonant changes in Portuguese include the weakening of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /b/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɡ/
       </span>
       to fricative
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [β]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ð]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɣ]
       </span>
       , while in BP these phonemes are maintained as stops in all positions. Portuguese consistently converts syllable-final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [z]
       </span>
       to palatal
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʒ]
       </span>
       , while most dialects of BP maintain them as dentals. Whether such a change happens in BP is highly variable according to dialect.
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       and a few states in the Northeast are particularly known for such pronunciation;
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       , on the other hand, along with most other Brazilian dialects, is particularly known for lacking it. In the Northeast, it is more likely to happen before a consonant than word-finally, and it varies from region to region. Some dialects (such as that of
       <a href="/wiki/Pernambuco" title="Pernambuco">
        Pernambuco
       </a>
       ) have the same pattern as Rio, while in several other dialects (such as that of
       <a href="/wiki/Cear%C3%A1" title="Ceará">
        Ceará
       </a>
       ), the palatal
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʒ]
       </span>
       replace
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s]
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [z]
       </span>
       only before the consonants
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       . A vowel change that does not occur in BP is the lowering of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /e/
       </span>
       to
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɐ]
       </span>
       before palatal sounds (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʃ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʒ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɲ]
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ʎ]
       </span>
       , and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       ) and in the diphthong
       <i>
        em
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ẽĩ/
       </span>
       , which merges with the diphthong
       <i>
        ãe
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɐ̃ĩ/
       </span>
       normally, but not in BP.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are many dialect-specific phonetic aspects in BP that can be essential characteristics of a dialect or another in
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazil
       </a>
       . For example, Cearense is notorious for changing
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [v]
       </span>
       into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [h]
       </span>
       in rapid speech (
       <i>
        vamos
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [vɐ̃mu]
       </span>
       , "let's go", becomes
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [hɐ̃mu]
       </span>
       ); more rural dialects in southeastern states, including São Paulo and Minas Gerais, change preconsonantal "r" into
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [ɹ]
       </span>
       ; several dialects reduce the diminutive suffix
       <i>
        inho
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        im
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        carrinho
       </i>
       , "little car:"
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kaˈxiɲu]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kaˈxi(ŋ)]
       </span>
       and several dialects nasalize the
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /d/
       </span>
       in the gerund form: "cantando"
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kɐ̃ˈtɐ̃du]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [kɐ̃ˈtɐ̃nu]
       </span>
       . Another common change that often makes the difference between two regions' dialects is the
       <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(phonetics)" title="Palatalization (phonetics)">
        palatalization
       </a>
       of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       followed by the vowel
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /i/
       </span>
       . Thus, there are two slightly distinct pronunciations of the word
       <i>
        menina
       </i>
       , "girl:" with palatalized
       <i>
        ni
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [miˈnʲinɐ]
       </span>
       and without palatalization
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [miˈninɐ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A change that is in the process of spreading in BP and perhaps started in the Northeast is the insertion of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [j]
       </span>
       after stressed vowels before
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /s/
       </span>
       at the end of a syllable. It began in the context of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /a/
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        mas
       </i>
       "but" is now pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [majs]
       </span>
       in most of Brazil, making it
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homophonous" title="Homophonous">
        homophonous
       </a>
       with
       <i>
        mais
       </i>
       "more"). Also, the change is spreading to other final vowels, and, at least in the Northeast and also Southeast, the normal pronunciations of
       <i>
        voz
       </i>
       "voice" is
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [vɔjs]
       </span>
       . Similarly,
       <i>
        três
       </i>
       "three" becomes
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tɾejs]
       </span>
       , making it rhyme with
       <i>
        seis
       </i>
       "six"
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [sejs]
       </span>
       ; that may explain the common Brazilian replacement of
       <i>
        seis
       </i>
       with
       <i>
        meia
       </i>
       ("half", as in "half a dozen") when phone numbers are spelled out.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Differences_in_the_informal_spoken_language">
        Differences in the informal spoken language
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Differences in the informal spoken language">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There are various differences between European Portuguese and Brazilian Portuguese, such as the dropping of the
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_verb_conjugation" title="Portuguese verb conjugation">
        second-person conjugations
       </a>
       (and, in some dialects, of the
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        second-person
       </a>
       pronoun itself) in everyday usage and the use of subject pronouns (
       <i>
        <span lang="pt" xml:lang="pt">
         ele, ela, eles, elas
        </span>
       </i>
       ) as direct objects.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar_2">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Spoken Brazilian Portuguese usage differs from Standard Portuguese usage.The differences include the placement of
       <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">
        clitic
       </a>
       pronouns and, in Brazil, the use of subject pronouns as objects in the third person. Nonstandard verb inflections are also common in colloquial Brazilian Portuguese.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Affirmation_and_negation">
        Affirmation and negation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Affirmation and negation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Spoken Portuguese rarely uses the affirmation adverb
       <b>
        sim
       </b>
       ("yes") in informal speech. Instead, the usual reply is a repetition of the verb of the question.
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        BP:
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Você foi na/à/pra biblioteca?
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Fui.
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       or
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Tu foste/foi na/à/pra biblioteca?
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Fui.
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       <b>
        Translation
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "Have you gone to the library yet?"
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "Yes, I went there yesterday."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In BP, it is common to form a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yes-no_question" title="Yes-no question">
        yes/no question
       </a>
       as a declarative sentence followed by the
       <a href="/wiki/Tag_question" title="Tag question">
        tag question
       </a>
       <i>
        não é?
       </i>
       ("isn't it?"), contracted in informal speech to
       <i>
        né?
       </i>
       (compare English "He is a teacher, isn't he?"). The affirmative answer to such a question is a repetition of the verb: "É:".
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        BP:
       </b>
      </p>
      <p>
       —
       <i>
        Ele não fez o que devia, né?
       </i>
       ("He didn't do what he should've, did he?")
      </p>
      <p>
       —
       <i>
        É.
       </i>
       ("Right, he didn't.")
      </p>
      <p>
       or
      </p>
      <p>
       —
       <i>
        Ela já foi atriz, né?
       </i>
       ("She had already been an actress, hadn't she?")
      </p>
      <p>
       —
       <i>
        É.
       </i>
       ("She already had.") Or –
       <i>
        É, sim, ela já foi.
       </i>
       (If a longer answer is preferred.)
      </p>
      <p>
       It is also common to negate statements twice for emphasis, with
       <i>
        não
       </i>
       ("no") before and after the verb:
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        BP:
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Você fala inglês?
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Não falo, não.
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "Do you speak English?"
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "I don't speak [it], no."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Sometimes, even a triple negative is possible:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Você fala inglês?
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Não. Não falo, não
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "Do you speak English?"
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "No. I don't speak it, no."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In some regions, the first "não" of a "não...não" pair is pronounced
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [nũ]
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In some cases, the redundancy of the first
       <i>
        não
       </i>
       results in its omission, which produces an apparent reversal of word order:
      </p>
      <p>
       <b>
        BP
       </b>
       :
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        — Você fala inglês?
       </i>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         — Falo não.
        </i>
        ("[I] speak not")
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       <b>
        Translation
       </b>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "Do you speak English?"
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "No, I don't."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Imperative">
        Imperative
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Imperative">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Standard Portuguese forms a command according to the
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        grammatical person
       </a>
       of the subject (who is ordered to do the action) by using either the imperative form of the verb or the present subjunctive. Thus, one should use different inflections according to the pronoun used as the subject:
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       ('you', the grammatical second person with the imperative form) or
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       ('you', the grammatical third person with the present subjunctive):
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Tu és burro, cal
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         a boca! (cala-te)
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Você é burro, cal
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         a boca! (cale-se)
        </i>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        "You are stupid, shut your mouth! (shut up)"
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Currently, several dialects of BP have largely lost the second-person pronouns, but even they use the second-person imperative in addition to the third-person present subjunctive form that should be used with
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       :
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        BP:
        <i>
         Você é burro, cal
         <b>
          e
         </b>
         a boca!
        </i>
        <b>
         OR
        </b>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        BP:
        <i>
         Você é burro, cal
         <b>
          a
         </b>
         a boca!
        </i>
        (considered grammatically incorrect, but completely dominant in informal language)
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Brazilian Portuguese uses the second-person imperative forms even when referring to
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       and not
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       , in the case of the verb
       <i>
        ser
       </i>
       'to be (permanently)' and
       <i>
        estar
       </i>
       'to be (temporarily)', the second-person imperative
       <i>
        sê
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        está
       </i>
       are never used; the third-person subjunctive forms
       <i>
        seja
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        esteja
       </i>
       may be used instead.
      </p>
      <p>
       The negative command forms use the
       <a href="/wiki/Subjunctive_mood" title="Subjunctive mood">
        subjunctive
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Present_tense" title="Present tense">
        present tense
       </a>
       forms of the verb. However, as for the second person forms, Brazilian Portuguese traditionally does not use the subjunctive-derived ones in spoken language. Instead, they employ the imperative forms: "Não anda", rather than the grammatically correct "Não andes".
      </p>
      <p>
       As for other grammatical persons, there is no such phenomenon because both the positive imperative and the negative imperative forms are from their respective present tense forms in the subjunctive mood:
       <i>
        Não jogue papel na grama
       </i>
       (Don't throw paper on the grass);
       <i>
        Não fume
       </i>
       (Don't smoke).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Deictics">
        Deictics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Deictics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In spoken Brazilian Portuguese, the first two adjectives/pronouns usually merge:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Esse
        </i>
        'this (one)' [near the speaker] / 'that (one)' [near the addressee]
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Aquele
        </i>
        'that (one)' [away from both]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Example:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Essa é minha camiseta nova.
        </i>
        (BP)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        This is my new T-shirt.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       Perhaps as a means of avoiding or clarifying some ambiguities created by the fact that "este" (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [st]
       </span>
       &gt;
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [s]
       </span>
       ) and "esse" have merged into the same word, informal BP often uses the demonstrative pronoun with some adverb that indicates its placement in relation to the addressee: if there are two skirts in a room and one says,
       <i>
        Pega essa saia para mim
       </i>
       (Take this skirt for me), there may be some doubt about which of them must be taken so one may say
       <i>
        Pega essa aí
       </i>
       (Take this one there near you") in the original sense of the use of "essa", or
       <i>
        Pega essa saia aqui
       </i>
       (Take this one here).
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_pronouns_and_possessives">
        Personal pronouns and possessives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Personal pronouns and possessives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_personal_pronouns" title="Portuguese personal pronouns">
        Portuguese personal pronouns
       </a>
      </div>
      <h5>
       <span id="Tu_and_voc.C3.AA">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tu_and_você">
        <i>
         Tu
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         você
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Tu and você">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In many dialects of BP,
       <b>
        você
       </b>
       (formal "you") replaces
       <b>
        tu
       </b>
       (informal "you"). The object pronoun, however, is still
       <b>
        te
       </b>
       (
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [tʃi] or [ti]
       </span>
       ). Also, other forms such as
       <i>
        teu
       </i>
       (possessive),
       <b>
        ti
       </b>
       (postprepositional), and
       <b>
        contigo
       </b>
       ("with you") are still common in most regions of Brazil, especially in areas in which
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is still frequent.
      </p>
      <p>
       Hence, the combination of object
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       with subject
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       in informal BP:
       <i>
        eu te disse para você ir
       </i>
       (I told you that you should go). In addition, in all the country, the imperative forms may also be the same as the formal second-person forms, but it is argued by some that it is the third-person singular indicative which doubles as the imperative:
       <i>
        fala o que você fez
       </i>
       instead of
       <i>
        fale o que você fez
       </i>
       ("say what you did").
      </p>
      <p>
       In areas in which
       <b>
        você
       </b>
       has largely replaced
       <b>
        tu
       </b>
       , the forms
       <i>
        ti/te
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        contigo
       </i>
       may be replaced by
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        com você
       </i>
       . Therefore, either
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       (following the verb) or
       <i>
        te
       </i>
       (preceding the verb) can be used as the object pronoun in informal BP. A speaker may thus end up saying "I love you" in two ways:
       <i>
        eu amo você
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        eu te amo
       </i>
       . In parts of the Northeast, most specifically in the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Piau%C3%AD" title="Piauí">
        Piauí
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pernambuco" title="Pernambuco">
        Pernambuco
       </a>
       , it is also common to use the indirect object pronoun
       <i>
        lhe
       </i>
       as a second-person object pronoun:
       <i>
        eu lhe amo
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In parts of the South, in most of the North and most of the Northeast, and in the city of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santos_(S%C3%A3o_Paulo)" title="Santos (São Paulo)">
        Santos
       </a>
       , the distinction between semi-formal ‘você' and familiar ’tu' is still maintained, and object and possessive pronouns pattern likewise. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">
        Paraná
       </a>
       state capital,
       <a href="/wiki/Curitiba" title="Curitiba">
        Curitiba
       </a>
       , 'tu' is not generally used.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       and minor parts of the Northeast (interior of some states and some speakers from the coast), both
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       (and associated object and possessive pronouns) are used interchangeably with little or no difference (sometimes even in the same sentence).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Salvador,_Bahia" title="Salvador, Bahia">
        Salvador
       </a>
       ,
       <b>
        tu
       </b>
       is never used and is replaced by
       <b>
        você
       </b>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Most Brazilians who use
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       use it with the third-person verb:
       <i>
        tu vai ao banco.
       </i>
       "Tu" with the second-person verb can still be found in
       <a href="/wiki/Maranh%C3%A3o" title="Maranhão">
        Maranhão
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pernambuco" title="Pernambuco">
        Pernambuco
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Piau%C3%AD" title="Piauí">
        Piauí
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">
        Santa Catarina
       </a>
       . A few cities in
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
        Rio Grande do Sul
       </a>
       (but in the rest of the state speakers may or may not use it in more formal speech), mainly near the border with
       <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">
        Uruguay
       </a>
       , have a slightly different pronunciation in some instances (
       <i>
        tu vieste
       </i>
       becomes
       <i>
        tu viesse
       </i>
       ), which is also present in
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Catarina_(state)" title="Santa Catarina (state)">
        Santa Catarina
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Pernambuco" title="Pernambuco">
        Pernambuco
       </a>
       . In the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Par%C3%A1" title="Pará">
        Pará
       </a>
       and Amazonas,
       <i>
        tu
       </i>
       is used much more often than
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       and is always accompanied by a second-person verb.
      </p>
      <p>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_City" title="São Paulo City">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       , the use of "tu" in print and conversation is no longer very common and is replaced by "você." However, São Paulo is now home to many immigrants of Northeastern origin, who may employ "tu" quite often in their everyday speech.
       <i>
        Você
       </i>
       is predominant in most of the Southeastern and Center Western regions; it is almost entirely prevalent in the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
        Minas Gerais
       </a>
       (apart from portions of the countryside, such as the region of São João da Ponte, where "tu" is also present
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and
       <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
        Espírito Santo
       </a>
       , but "tu" is frequent in
       <a href="/wiki/Santos,_S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="Santos, São Paulo">
        Santos
       </a>
       and all coastal region of São Paulo state as well as some cities in the countryside.
      </p>
      <p>
       In most of Brazil "você" is often reduced to even more contracted forms, resulting
       <i>
        ocê
       </i>
       (mostly in the
       <a href="/wiki/Caipira_dialect" title="Caipira dialect">
        Caipira dialect
       </a>
       ) and, especially,
       <i>
        cê
       </i>
       because
       <i>
        vo-
       </i>
       is an unstressed syllable and so is dropped in rapid speech.
      </p>
      <h5>

      </h5>
