      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Third-person_direct_object_pronouns">
        Third-person direct object pronouns
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Third-person direct object pronouns">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In spoken informal registers of BP, the third-person object pronouns 'o', 'a', 'os', and 'as' are virtually nonexistent and are simply left out or, when necessary and usually only when referring to people, replaced by stressed subject pronouns like
       <i>
        ele
       </i>
       "he" or
       <i>
        isso
       </i>
       "that":
       <i>
        Eu vi ele
       </i>
       "I saw him" rather than
       <i>
        Eu o vi
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Seu_and_dele">
        <i>
         Seu
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         dele
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Seu and dele">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       When
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       is strictly a second-person pronoun, the use of possessive
       <i>
        seu/sua
       </i>
       may turn some phrases quite ambiguous since one would wonder whether seu/sua refers to the second person
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       or to the third person
       <i>
        ele/ela
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       BP thus tends to use the third-person possessive 'seu' to mean "your" since
       <i>
        você
       </i>
       is a third-person pronoun and uses 'dele', 'dela', 'deles', and 'delas' ("of him/her/them" and placed after the noun) as third-person possessive forms. If no ambiguity could arise (especially in narrative texts),
       <i>
        seu
       </i>
       is also used to mean 'his' or 'her'.
      </p>
      <p>
       Both forms ('seu' or 'dele(s) /dela(s)') are considered grammatically correct in Brazilian Portuguese.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definite_article_before_possessive">
        Definite article before possessive
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Definite article before possessive">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       In Portuguese, one may or may not include the definite article before a possessive pronoun (
       <i>
        meu livro
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        o meu livro
       </i>
       , for instance). The variants of use in each dialect of Portuguese are mostly a matter of preference: it does not usually mean a dialect completely abandoned either form.
      </p>
      <p>
       In Southeastern Brazilian Portuguese, especially in the standard dialects of the cities of
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">
        São Paulo
       </a>
       , the definite article is normally used as in Portugal, but many speakers do not use it at the beginning of the sentence or in titles:
       <i>
        Minha novela
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Meu tio matou um cara
       </i>
       . In Northeastern BP dialects and in Central and Northern parts of the state of
       <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">
        Rio de Janeiro
       </a>
       , (starting from
       <a href="/wiki/Niter%C3%B3i" title="Niterói">
        Niterói
       </a>
       ), rural parts of
       <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
        Minas Gerais
       </a>
       , and all over
       <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
        Espírito Santo
       </a>
       State, speakers tend to but do not always drop the definite article, but both
       <i>
        esse é o meu gato
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        esse é meu gato
       </i>
       are likely in speech.
      </p>
      <p>
       Formal written Brazilian Portuguese tends, however, to omit the definite article in accordance with prescriptive grammar rules derived from Classical Portuguese even if the alternative form is also considered correct, but many teachers consider it inelegant.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Syntax_2">
        Syntax
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Syntax">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       Some of the examples on the right side of the table below are colloquial or regional in Brazil. Literal translations are provided to illustrate how word order changes between varieties.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
        </th>
        <th>
         Brazilian Portuguese
         <br/>
         (formal)
        </th>
        <th>
         Brazilian Portuguese
         <br/>
         (colloquial)
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td rowspan="2">
         <b>
          placement of
         </b>
         <br/>
         <b>
          clitic pronouns
         </b>
        </td>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;">
         <i>
          Eu te amo.
         </i>
         <p>
          "I you/thee love."
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <i>
          Responda-me!
         </i>
         (
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         )
         <p>
          "Answer me!" (you)
         </p>
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          Me responda!
         </i>
         (
         <i>
          você
         </i>
         )
         <sup>
          1
         </sup>
         <p>
          "Me to answer!" (you)
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <b>
          use of personal
         </b>
         <br/>
         <b>
          pronouns
         </b>
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          Eu a vi.
         </i>
         <p>
          "I her saw."
         </p>
        </td>
        <td align="center">
         <i>
          Eu vi ela.
         </i>
         <p>
          "I saw she."
         </p>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Word order in the first Brazilian Portugues example is frequent in European Portuguese. Similar to the subordinate clauses like
       <i>
        Sabes
        <b>
         que eu te amo
        </b>
       </i>
       "You know
       <i>
        that I love you
       </i>
       ", but not in simple sentences like "I love you." However, in Portugal, an object pronoun would never be placed at the start of a sentence, as in the second example. The example in the bottom row of the table, with its deletion of "redundant" inflections, is considered ungrammatical, but it is nonetheless dominant in Brazil throughout all social classes.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_prepositions">
        Use of prepositions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Use of prepositions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Just as in the case of English, whose various dialects sometimes use different prepositions with the same verbs or nouns (
       <i>
        stand in/on line, in/on the street
       </i>
       ), BP usage sometimes requires prepositions that would not be normally used in Portugues for the same context.
      </p>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chamar_de">
        <i>
         Chamar de
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Chamar de">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       <i>
        Chamar
       </i>
       'call' is normally used with the preposition
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       in BP, especially when it means 'to describe someone as':
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Chamei ele de ladrão.
        </i>
        (BP)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        I called him a thief.
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Em_with_verbs_of_movement">
        <i>
         Em
        </i>
        with verbs of movement
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Em with verbs of movement">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <p>
       When movement to a place is described, BP uses
       <i>
        em
       </i>
       (contracted with an article, if necessary):
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Fui na praça.
        </i>
        (BP)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        I went to the square. [temporarily]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In BP, the preposition
       <i>
        para
       </i>
       can also be used with such verbs with no difference in meaning:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Fui para a praça.
        </i>
        (BP)
       </dd>
       <dd>
        I went to the square. [definitively]
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">
        Dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <ol>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Caipira_dialect" title="Caipira dialect">
          Caipira
         </a>
        </i>
        — Spoken in the states of
        <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
         São Paulo
        </a>
        (mostly in the countryside and rural areas); southern
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
         Minas Gerais
        </a>
        , northern
        <a href="/wiki/Paran%C3%A1_(state)" title="Paraná (state)">
         Paraná
        </a>
        and southeastern
        <a href="/wiki/Mato_Grosso_do_Sul" title="Mato Grosso do Sul">
         Mato Grosso do Sul
        </a>
        . Depending on the vision of what constitutes
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Tri%C3%A2ngulo_Mineiro" title="Triângulo Mineiro">
         Triângulo Mineiro
        </a>
        , border areas of
        <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A1s" title="Goiás">
         Goiás
        </a>
        and the remaining parts of Mato Grosso do Sul are included, and the frontier of
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        in Minas Gerais is expanded further northerly, though not reaching metropolitan
        <a href="/wiki/Belo_Horizonte" title="Belo Horizonte">
         Belo Horizonte
        </a>
        . It is often said that
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        appeared by
        <a href="/wiki/Decreolization" title="Decreolization">
         decreolization
        </a>
        of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Tupi" title="Old Tupi">
         língua brasílica
        </a>
        and the related
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L%C3%ADngua_geral_paulista" title="Língua geral paulista">
         língua geral paulista
        </a>
        , then spoken in almost all of what is now São Paulo, a former
        <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
         lingua franca
        </a>
        in most of the contemporary
        <a href="/wiki/Centro-Sul" title="Centro-Sul">
         Centro-Sul
        </a>
        of Brazil before the 18th century, brought by the
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bandeirante" title="Bandeirante">
          bandeirantes
         </a>
        </i>
        , interior pioneers of
        <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Brazil" title="Colonial Brazil">
         Colonial Brazil
        </a>
        , closely related to its northern counterpart
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nheengatu_language" title="Nheengatu language">
         Nheengatu
        </a>
        , and that is why the dialect shows many general differences from other variants of the language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
         <a href="#cite_note-30">
          [30]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It has striking remarkable differences in comparison to other Brazilian dialects in phonology, prosody and grammar, often
        <a href="/wiki/Social_stigma" title="Social stigma">
         stigmatized
        </a>
        as being strongly associated with a
        <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">
         substandard variant
        </a>
        , now mostly rural.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
         <a href="#cite_note-31">
          [31]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
         <a href="#cite_note-32">
          [32]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
         <a href="#cite_note-33">
          [33]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
         <a href="#cite_note-34">
          [34]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
         <a href="#cite_note-35">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/North_coast_Portuguese" title="North coast Portuguese">
         <i>
          Cearense
         </i>
         or
         <i>
          Costa norte
         </i>
        </a>
        — is a dialect spoken more sharply in the states of Ceará and Piauí. The variant of Ceará includes fairly distinctive traits it shares with the one spoken in Piauí, though, such as distinctive regional phonology and vocabulary (for example, a debuccalization process stronger than that of Portuguese, a different system of the vowel harmony that spans Brazil from
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         mineiro
        </i>
        to
        <i>
         amazofonia
        </i>
        but is especially prevalent in
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , a very coherent coda sibilant palatalization as those of Portugal and Rio de Janeiro but allowed in fewer environments than in other accents of
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , a greater presence of dental stop palatalization to palato-alveolar in comparison to other accents of
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , among others, as well as a great number of archaic Portuguese words).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
         <a href="#cite_note-36">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
         <a href="#cite_note-37">
          [37]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
         <a href="#cite_note-38">
          [38]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
         <a href="#cite_note-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
         <a href="#cite_note-40">
          [40]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
         <a href="#cite_note-41">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Baiano
        </i>
        — Found in
        <a href="/wiki/Bahia" title="Bahia">
         Bahia
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Sergipe" title="Sergipe">
         Sergipe
        </a>
        , northern Minas Gerais and border regions with
        <a href="/wiki/Goi%C3%A1s" title="Goiás">
         Goiás
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Tocantins" title="Tocantins">
         Tocantins
        </a>
        . Similar to
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        , it has a very characteristic
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_timing" title="Stress timing">
         syllable-timed rhythm
        </a>
        and the greatest tendency to pronounce unstressed vowels as open-mid
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">
          ɛ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         [
         <a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">
          ɔ
         </a>
         ]
        </span>
        .
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png">
           <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="915" data-file-width="885" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png/220px-Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png/330px-Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png/440px-Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png 2x" width="220"/>
          </a>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Portugueselanguagedialects-Brazil.png" title="Enlarge">
            </a>
           </div>
           <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
            Variants
           </a>
           and
           <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
            sociolects
           </a>
           of Brazilian Portuguese.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loudspeaker.svg">
         <img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </a>
        <a class="external text" href="http://cvc.instituto-camoes.pt/hlp/geografia/som90.html" rel="nofollow">
         <i>
          Fluminense
         </i>
        </a>
        — A broad dialect with many variants spoken in the states of
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro_(state)" title="Rio de Janeiro (state)">
         Rio de Janeiro
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Esp%C3%ADrito_Santo" title="Espírito Santo">
         Espírito Santo
        </a>
        and neighbouring eastern regions of
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
         Minas Gerais
        </a>
        .
        <i>
         Fluminense
        </i>
        formed in these previously
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        -speaking areas due to the gradual influence of European migrants, causing many people to distance their speech from their original dialect and incorporate new terms.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
         <a href="#cite_note-42">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <i>
         Fluminense
        </i>
        is sometimes referred to as
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        , however
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        is a more specific term referring to the accent of the
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Greater Rio de Janeiro">
         Greater Rio de Janeiro
        </a>
        area by speakers with a
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        dialect.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Gaúcho
        </i>
        — in
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
         Rio Grande do Sul
        </a>
        , similar to
        <i>
         sulista
        </i>
        . There are many distinct accents in Rio Grande do Sul, mainly due to the heavy influx of European immigrants of diverse origins who have settled in colonies throughout the state, and to the proximity to
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hispanosphere" title="Hispanosphere">
         Spanish-speaking nations
        </a>
        . The
        <i>
         gaúcho
        </i>
        word in itself is a Spanish
        <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">
         loanword
        </a>
        into Portuguese of obscure
        <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">
         Indigenous Amerindian
        </a>
        origins.
        <div class="thumb tright">
         <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Falantes_no_mundo.jpg">
           <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="408" data-file-width="513" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Falantes_no_mundo.jpg/200px-Falantes_no_mundo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Falantes_no_mundo.jpg/300px-Falantes_no_mundo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Falantes_no_mundo.jpg/400px-Falantes_no_mundo.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
          </a>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <div class="magnify">
            <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Falantes_no_mundo.jpg" title="Enlarge">
            </a>
           </div>
           Percentage of worldwide Portuguese speakers per country.
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Mineiro" title="Mineiro">
          Mineiro
         </a>
        </i>
        —
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais" title="Minas Gerais">
         Minas Gerais
        </a>
        (not prevalent in the
        <a href="/wiki/Tri%C3%A2ngulo_Mineiro" title="Triângulo Mineiro">
         Triângulo Mineiro
        </a>
        ). As the
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        area, its associated region was formerly a sparsely populated land where
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        was spoken, but
        <a href="/wiki/Minas_Gerais#History" title="Minas Gerais">
         the discovery of gold and gems made it the most prosperous Brazilian region
        </a>
        , what attracted Portuguese colonists, commoners from other parts of Brazil and their African slaves. South-southwestern,
        <a href="/wiki/Zona_da_Mata_(Minas_Gerais)" title="Zona da Mata (Minas Gerais)">
         southeastern
        </a>
        and northern areas of the state have fairly distinctive speech, actually approximating to
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        (popularly called, often pejoratively,
        <i>
         carioca do brejo
        </i>
        , "marsh carioca") and
        <i>
         baiano
        </i>
        respectively. Areas including and surrounding
        <a href="/wiki/Belo_Horizonte" title="Belo Horizonte">
         Belo Horizonte
        </a>
        have a distinctive accent.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loudspeaker.svg">
         <img alt="Loudspeaker.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
        </a>
        <a class="external text" href="http://cvc.instituto-camoes.pt/hlp/geografia/som91.html" rel="nofollow">
         <i>
          Nordestino
         </i>
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_43-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-43">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
        — more marked in the
        <a href="/wiki/Sert%C3%A3o" title="Sertão">
         Sertão
        </a>
        (7), where, in the 19th and 20th centuries and especially in the area including and surrounding the
        <i>
         sertão
        </i>
        (the dry land after
        <a href="/wiki/Agreste" title="Agreste">
         Agreste
        </a>
        ) of Pernambuco and southern Ceará, it could sound less comprehensible to speakers of other Portuguese dialects than Galician or
        <a href="/wiki/Rioplatense_Spanish" title="Rioplatense Spanish">
         Rioplatense Spanish
        </a>
        , and nowadays less distinctive from other variants in the metropolitan cities
        <a href="/wiki/Zona_da_Mata" title="Zona da Mata">
         along the coasts
        </a>
        . It can be divided in two regional variants, one that includes the northern
        <a href="/wiki/Maranh%C3%A3o" title="Maranhão">
         Maranhão
        </a>
        and southern of
        <a href="/wiki/Piau%C3%AD" title="Piauí">
         Piauí
        </a>
        , and other that goes from
        <a href="/wiki/Cear%C3%A1" title="Ceará">
         Ceará
        </a>
        to
        <a href="/wiki/Alagoas" title="Alagoas">
         Alagoas
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Nortista
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         amazofonia
        </i>
        — Most of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">
         Amazon Basin
        </a>
        states i.e.
        <a href="/wiki/North_Region,_Brazil" title="North Region, Brazil">
         Northern Brazil
        </a>
        . Before the 20th century, most people from the
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        area fleeing the droughts and their associated poverty settled here, so it has some similarities with the Portuguese dialect there spoken. The speech in and around the cities of
        <a href="/wiki/Bel%C3%A9m" title="Belém">
         Belém
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Manaus" title="Manaus">
         Manaus
        </a>
        has a more European flavor in phonology, prosody and grammar.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Paulistano
        </i>
        — Variants spoken around
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="Greater São Paulo">
         Greater São Paulo
        </a>
        in its maximum definition and more easterly areas of São Paulo state, as well perhaps "educated speech" from anywhere in
        <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state)" title="São Paulo (state)">
         the state of São Paulo
        </a>
        (where it coexists with
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ).
        <i>
         Caipira
        </i>
        is the hinterland sociolect of much of the
        <a href="/wiki/Centro-Sul" title="Centro-Sul">
         Central-Southern half
        </a>
        of Brazil, nowadays conservative only in the rural areas and associated with them, that has a historically
        <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">
         low prestige
        </a>
        in cities as Rio de Janeiro, Curitiba, Belo Horizonte, and until some years ago, in São Paulo itself.
        <a href="/wiki/Sociolinguistics" title="Sociolinguistics">
         Sociolinguistics
        </a>
        , or what by times is described as '
        <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_discrimination" title="Linguistic discrimination">
         linguistic prejudice
        </a>
        ', often correlated with
        <a href="/wiki/Class_discrimination" title="Class discrimination">
         classism
        </a>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-46">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
        is a polemic topic in the entirety of the country since the times of
        <a href="/wiki/Adoniran_Barbosa#Musical_production" title="Adoniran Barbosa">
         Adoniran Barbosa
        </a>
        . Also, the "Paulistano" accent was heavily influenced by the presence of immigrants in the city of São Paulo, especially the Italians.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Sertanejo
        </i>
        —
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Center-West_Region,_Brazil" title="Center-West Region, Brazil">
         Center-Western states
        </a>
        , and also much of
        <a href="/wiki/Tocantins" title="Tocantins">
         Tocantins
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Rond%C3%B4nia" title="Rondônia">
         Rondônia
        </a>
        . It is closer to
        <i>
         mineiro
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         nortista
        </i>
        depending on the location.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Sulista
        </i>
        — The variants spoken in the areas between the northern regions of
        <a href="/wiki/Rio_Grande_do_Sul" title="Rio Grande do Sul">
         Rio Grande do Sul
        </a>
        and southern regions of São Paulo state, encompassing most of
        <a href="/wiki/South_Region,_Brazil" title="South Region, Brazil">
         southern Brazil
        </a>
        . The city of
        <a href="/wiki/Curitiba" title="Curitiba">
         Curitiba
        </a>
        does have a fairly distinct accent as well, and a relative majority of speakers around and in
        <a href="/wiki/Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Florianópolis">
         Florianópolis
        </a>
        also speak this variant (many speak
        <i>
         florianopolitano
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         manezinho da ilha
        </i>
        instead, related to the European Portuguese dialects spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">
         Azores
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">
         Madeira
        </a>
        ). Speech of northern Paraná is closer to that of inland São Paulo.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Florianopolitan_dialect" title="Florianopolitan dialect">
          Florianopolitano
         </a>
        </i>
        — Variants heavily influenced by European Portuguese spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Florianópolis">
         Florianópolis
        </a>
        city (due to a heavy immigration movement from Portugal, mainly its
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autonomous_regions_of_Portugal" title="Autonomous regions of Portugal">
         insular regions
        </a>
        ) and much of its metropolitan area,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grande_Florian%C3%B3polis" title="Grande Florianópolis">
         Grande Florianópolis
        </a>
        , said to be a continuum between those whose speech most resemble
        <i>
         sulista
        </i>
        dialects and those whose speech most resemble
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        and European ones, called, often pejoratively,
        <i>
         manezinho da ilha
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Carioca" title="Carioca">
          Carioca
         </a>
        </i>
        — Not a dialect, but
        <a href="/wiki/Sociolect" title="Sociolect">
         sociolects
        </a>
        of the
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        variant spoken in an area roughly corresponding to
        <a href="/wiki/Greater_Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Greater Rio de Janeiro">
         Greater Rio de Janeiro
        </a>
        . It appeared after locals came in contact with the Portuguese aristocracy amidst the
        <a href="/wiki/Transfer_of_the_Portuguese_Court_to_Brazil" title="Transfer of the Portuguese Court to Brazil">
         Portuguese royal family fled
        </a>
        in the early 19th century. There is actually a continuum between Vernacular countryside accents and the
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        sociolect, and the educated speech (in Portuguese
        <i>
         norma culta
        </i>
        , which most closely resembles other Brazilian Portuguese standards but with marked recent Portuguese influences, the nearest ones among the country's dialects along
        <i>
         florianopolitano
        </i>
        ), so that not all people native to the state of Rio de Janeiro speak the said sociolect, but most
        <i>
         carioca
        </i>
        speakers will use the standard variant not influenced by it that is rather uniform around Brazil depending on context (emphasis or formality, for example).
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Brasiliense
        </i>
        — used in
        <a href="/wiki/Bras%C3%ADlia" title="Brasília">
         Brasília
        </a>
        and its metropolitan area.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        It is not considered a dialect, but more of a regional variant – often deemed to be closer to
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        than the dialect commonly spoken in most of Goiás,
        <i>
         sertanejo
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Arco do desflorestamento
        </i>
        or
        <i>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://pt.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dialeto_da_serra_amaz%C3%B4nica" title="pt:Dialeto da serra amazônica">
          serra amazônica
         </a>
        </i>
        — Known in its region as the "accent of the migrants", it has similarities with
        <i>
         caipira
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         sertanejo
        </i>
        and often
        <i>
         sulista
        </i>
        that make it differing from
        <i>
         amazofonia
        </i>
        (in the opposite group of Brazilian dialects, in which it is placed along
        <i>
         nordestino
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         baiano
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         mineiro
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         fluminense
        </i>
        ). It is the most recent dialect, which appeared by the settlement of families from various other Brazilian regions attracted by the cheap land offer in recently
        <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">
         deforested
        </a>
        areas.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Recifense
        </i>
        — used in
        <a href="/wiki/Recife" title="Recife">
         Recife
        </a>
        and its metropolitan area.
       </li>
      </ol>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Diglossia">
        Diglossia
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Diglossia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       According to some contemporary
       <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">
        Brazilian
       </a>
       linguists (Bortoni, Kato, Mattos e Silva, Perini and most recently, with great impact, Bagno), Brazilian Portuguese may be a highly
       <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
        diglossic language
       </a>
       . This theory claims that there is an L-variant (termed "Brazilian Vernacular"), which would be the mother tongue of all Brazilians, and an H-variant (standard Brazilian Portuguese) acquired through schooling. L-variant represents a simplified form of the language (in terms of grammar, but not of phonetics) that could have evolved from 16th-century Portuguese, influenced by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerindian" title="Amerindian">
        Amerindian
       </a>
       (mostly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tupi_Antigo" title="Tupi Antigo">
        Tupi
       </a>
       ) and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_languages" title="African languages">
        African languages
       </a>
       , while H-variant would be based on 19th-century European Portuguese (and very similar to Standard European Portuguese, with only minor differences in
       <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
        spelling
       </a>
       and grammar usage). Mário A. Perini, a Brazilian linguist, even compares the depth of the differences between L- and H- variants of Brazilian Portuguese with those between Standard Spanish and European Portuguese. However, his proposal is not widely accepted by either grammarians or academics. Milton M. Azevedo wrote a chapter on diglossia in his monograph:
       <i>
        Portuguese language (A linguistic introduction)
       </i>
       , published by
       <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">
        Cambridge University Press
       </a>
       in 2005.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Usage">
        Usage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Usage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       From this point of view, the L-variant is the spoken form of Brazilian Portuguese, which should be avoided only in very formal speech (court interrogation, political debate) while the H-variant is the written form of Brazilian Portuguese, avoided only in informal writing (such as songs lyrics, love letters, intimate friends correspondence). Even language professors frequently use the L-variant while explaining students the structure and usage of the H-variant; in essays, nevertheless, all students are expected to use H-variant.
      </p>
      <p>
       The L-variant may be used in songs, movies, soap operas, sitcoms and other television shows, although, at times, the H-variant is used in historic films or soap operas to make the language used sound more ‘elegant’ or ‘archaic’. There is a claim that the H-variant used to be preferred when dubbing foreign films and series into Brazilian Portuguese,
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       but nowadays the L-variant is preferred, although this seems to lack evidence. Movie subtitles normally use a mixture of L- and H-variants, but remain closer to the H-variant.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most literary works are written in the H-variant. There would have been attempts at writing in the L-variant (such as the masterpiece
       <i>
        Macunaíma
       </i>
       , written by Brazilian modernist
       <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A1rio_de_Andrade" title="Mário de Andrade">
        Mário de Andrade
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        Grande Sertão: Veredas
       </i>
       , by João
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guimar%C3%A3es_Rosa" title="Guimarães Rosa">
        Guimarães Rosa
       </a>
       ), but, presently, the L-variant is claimed to be used only in dialogue. Still, many contemporary writers like using the H-variant even in informal dialogue. This is also true of translated books, which never use the L-variant, only the H one. Children's books seem to be more L-friendly, but, again, if they are translated from another language (
       <i>
        The Little Prince
       </i>
       , for instance) they will use the H-variant only.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2007)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Prestige">
        Prestige
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Prestige">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       This theory also posits that the matter of diglossia in Brazil is further complicated by forces of political and cultural bias, though those are not clearly named. Language is sometimes a tool of social exclusion or social choice.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2010)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mário A. Perini, a Brazilian linguist, has said:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "There are two languages in Brazil. The one we write (and which is called "Portuguese"), and another one that we speak (which is so despised that there is not a name to call it). The latter is the mother tongue of Brazilians, the former has to be learned in school, and a majority of population does not manage to master it appropriately.... Personally, I do not object to us writing Portuguese, but I think it is important to make clear that Portuguese is (at least in Brazil) only a written language. Our mother tongue is not Portuguese, but Brazilian Vernacular. This is not a slogan, nor a political statement, it is simply recognition of a fact.... There are linguistic teams working hard in order to give the full description of the structure of the Vernacular. So, there are hopes, that within some years, we will have appropriate grammars of our mother tongue, the language that has been ignored, denied and despised for such a long time."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       According to Milton M. Azevedo (Brazilian linguist):
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        "The relationship between Vernacular Brazilian Portuguese and the formal prescriptive variety fulfills the basic conditions of Ferguson's definition [of diglossia]...[...] Considering the difficulty encountered by vernacular speakers to acquire the standard, an understanding of those relationships appears to have broad educational significance. The teaching of Portuguese has traditionally meant imparting a prescriptive formal standard based on a literary register (Cunha 1985: 24) that is often at variance with the language with which students are familiar. As in a diglossic situation, vernacular speakers must learn to read and write in a dialect they neither speak nor fully understand, a circumstance that may have a bearing on the high dropout rate in elementary schools..."
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       According to Bagno (1999) the two variants coexist and intermingle quite seamlessly, but their status is not clear-cut. Brazilian Vernacular is still frowned upon by most grammarians and language teachers, with only remarkably few linguists championing its cause. Some of this minority, of which Bagno is an example, appeal to their readers by their ideas that grammarians would be detractors of the termed Brazilian Vernacular, by naming it a "corrupt" form of the "pure" standard, an attitude which they classify as "linguistic prejudice". Their arguments include the postulate that the Vernacular form simplifies some of the intricacies of standard Portuguese (verbal conjugation, pronoun handling, plural forms, etc.).
      </p>
      <p>
       Bagno denounces the prejudice against the vernacular in what he terms the "8 Myths":
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        There is a striking uniformity in Brazilian Portuguese
       </li>
       <li>
        A big amount of Brazilians speak Portuguese poorly while in Portugal people speak it very well
       </li>
       <li>
        Portuguese is difficult to learn and speak
       </li>
       <li>
        People that have had poor education can't speak anything correctly
       </li>
       <li>
        In the state of Maranhão people speak a better Portuguese than elsewhere in Brazil
       </li>
       <li>
        We should speak as closely as possible to the written language
       </li>
       <li>
        The knowledge of grammar is essential to the correct and proper use of a language
       </li>
       <li>
        To master Standard Portuguese is the path to social promotion
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       In opposition to the "myths", Bagno counters that:
      </p>
      <ol>
       <li>
        The uniformity of Brazilian Portuguese is just about what linguistics would predict for such a large country whose population has not, generally, been literate for centuries and which has experienced considerable foreign influence, that is, this uniformity is more apparent than real.
       </li>
       <li>
        Brazilians speak Standard Portuguese poorly because they speak a language that is sufficiently different from Standard Portuguese so that the latter sounds almost "foreign" to them. In terms of comparison, it is easier for many Brazilians to understand someone from a Spanish-speaking South American country than someone from Portugal because the spoken varieties of Portuguese on either side of the Atlantic have diverged to the point of nearly being mutually unintelligible.
       </li>
       <li>
        No language is difficult for those who speak it. Difficulty appears when two conditions are met: the standard language diverges from the vernacular and a speaker of the vernacular tries to learn the standard version. This divergence is the precise reason why spelling and grammar reforms happen every now and then.
       </li>
       <li>
        People with less education can speak the vernacular or often several varieties of the vernacular, and they speak it well. They might, however, have trouble in speaking Standard Portuguese, but this is due to lack of experience rather than to any inherent deficiency in their linguistic mastery.
       </li>
       <li>
        The people of Maranhão are not generally better than fellow Brazilians from other states in speaking Standard Portuguese, especially because that state is one of the poorest and has one of the lowest literacy rates.
       </li>
       <li>
        It is the written language that must reflect the spoken and not vice versa: it is not the tail that wags the dog.
       </li>
       <li>
        The knowledge of grammar is intuitive for those who speak their native languages. Problems arise when they begin to study the grammar of a foreign language.
       </li>
       <li>
        Rich and influential people themselves often do not follow the grammatical rules of Standard Portuguese. Standard Portuguese is mostly a jewel or shibboleth for powerless middle-class careers (journalists, teachers, writers, actors, etc.).
       </li>
      </ol>
      <p>
       Whether Bagno's points are valid or not is open to debate, especially the solutions he recommends for the problems he claims to have identified. Whereas some agree that he has captured the feelings of the Brazilians towards Brazil's linguistic situation well, his book (
       <i>
        Linguistic Prejudice: What it Is, What To Do
       </i>
       ) has been heavily criticized by some linguists and grammarians, due to his unorthodox claims, sometimes asserted to be biased or unproven.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">
        Impact
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Impact">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The cultural influence of Brazilian Portuguese in the rest of the Portuguese-speaking world has greatly increased in the last decades of the 20th century, due to the popularity of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brazilian_music" title="Brazilian music">
        Brazilian music
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Telenovela" title="Telenovela">
        Brazilian soap operas
       </a>
       . Since Brazil joined
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mercosul" title="Mercosul">
        Mercosul
       </a>
       , the South American free trade zone, Portuguese has been increasingly studied as a
       <a href="/wiki/Foreign_language" title="Foreign language">
        foreign language
       </a>
       in Spanish-speaking partner countries.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2009)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many words of Brazilian origin (also used in other Portuguese-speaking countries) have also entered into
       <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
        English
       </a>
       :
       <a href="/wiki/Samba" title="Samba">
        samba
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bossa_nova" title="Bossa nova">
        bossa nova
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_cruzeiro" title="Brazilian cruzeiro">
        cruzeiro
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milreis" title="Milreis">
        milreis
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Capoeira" title="Capoeira">
        capoeira
       </a>
       . While originally Angolan, the word "samba" only became famous worldwide because of its popularity in Brazil.
      </p>
      <p>
       After independence in 1822, Brazilian idioms with African and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amerindian" title="Amerindian">
        Amerindian
       </a>
       influences were brought to Portugal by returning
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_Brazilian" title="Portuguese Brazilian">
        Portuguese Brazilians
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        luso-brasileiros
       </i>
       in Portuguese).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Language_codes">
        Language codes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Language codes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <code>
        pt
       </code>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Language_code" title="Language code">
        language code
       </a>
       for
       <i>
        Portuguese
       </i>
       , defined by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_standard" title="ISO standard">
        ISO standards
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-1" title="ISO 639-1">
        ISO 639-1
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/ISO_3166-1_alpha-2" title="ISO 3166-1 alpha-2">
        ISO 3166-1 alpha-2
       </a>
       ). There is no ISO code for spoken or written Brazilian Portuguese.
      </p>
      <p>
       <code>
        bzs
       </code>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Language_code" title="Language code">
        language code
       </a>
       for the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Sign_Language" title="Brazilian Sign Language">
         Brazilian Sign Language
        </a>
       </i>
       , defined by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_standard" title="ISO standard">
        ISO standards
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">
        ISO 639-3
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <code>
        pt-BR
       </code>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Language_code" title="Language code">
        language code
       </a>
       for the
       <i>
        Brazilian Portuguese
       </i>
       , defined by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internet_standard" title="Internet standard">
        Internet standards
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/IETF_language_tag" title="IETF language tag">
        IETF language tag
       </a>
       ).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Brazilian_Portuguese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: See also">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>

      <div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">
       <ul style="display:table;box-sizing:border-box;padding:0.1em;max-width:175px;background:#f9f9f9;font-size:85%;line-height:110%;font-style:italic;font-weight:bold">

          Note: the speaker of this sound file is from Rio de Janeiro, and he is talking about his experience with
          <i>
           nordestino
          </i>
          and
          <i>
           nortista
          </i>
          accents.
         </span>
        </li>

             <li>
              <i>
               Italics
              </i>
              indicate
              <a href="/wiki/Language_death" title="Language death">
               extinct languages
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              <b>
               Bold
              </b>
              indicates
              <a href="/wiki/List_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="List of languages by number of native speakers">
               languages with more than 5 million speakers
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              Languages between parentheses are
              <a href="/wiki/Variety_(linguistics)" title="Variety (linguistics)">
               varieties
              </a>
              of the language on their left.
             </li>
